# Jessica Campbell Jones  —  *Jewel (retired); Knightress (briefly); Power Woman (briefly)*

![[_attachments/jessica_jones.png]]

# Jessica Jones — UATU INTELLIGENCE DOSSIER

*Full-existence intelligence briefing. Independent, evidence-based assessment — the benevolent inverse of total surveillance.*


## Executive Brief (BLUF)

# EXECUTIVE BRIEF (BLUF)

**Subject:** Jones, Jessica Campbell. DOB: pre-incident adolescence in Forest Hills, Queens. Operating base: Alias Investigations, varying Manhattan addresses (Hell's Kitchen and lower-midtown over the file's life). Marital: married to Cage, Luke (intermittent estrangements, no dissolution). Dependents: one (Cage-Jones, Danielle, minor). Powers-registered status: reluctantly compliant, minimally cooperative.

---

## BLUF — One Paragraph

Jessica Jones is a chronically traumatized, high-functioning, morally rigorous adult survivor of prolonged coercive captivity, operating a small private-investigations practice as both livelihood and self-medication, who has organized her entire post-trauma existence around a single non-negotiable axiom — *no one else gets to author my will* — and a single reparative project — *I will stand between predators and the people they would otherwise reach.* She is not a hero in the costumed sense and refuses the label with corrosive consistency; she is, in operational terms, a one-woman protective service who works drunk, alone, and at unsociable hours, and who has rebuilt a self out of competence at exactly the kind of work that her own catastrophe should have made impossible. Threats to her child, to her husband, or anything stimulus-coded to her captor will decompensate her predictably; everything else she absorbs.

---

## The Through-Line

The single longitudinal thread that organizes every era of the subject's life is **the management of will under conditions where will has been demonstrated, repeatedly, to be revocable by others**. Read in sequence — the highway collision that erased her family without her consent, the chemical exposure that rewrote her body without her consent, the adoption that re-housed her without her consent, the captivity that took her cognition and sexuality without her consent, the Avenger beating delivered to her under the belief she was acting on her own behalf when she was not — and the entire biography becomes intelligible as a series of *unconsented impositions* against which the adult subject has constructed a counter-architecture of obsessive, even self-defeating, autonomy.

This is why she drinks alone, works alone, investigates alone, goes undercover without telling her husband, refuses psychiatric care, refuses the costume, refuses the team, refuses the praise, refuses the help. Each refusal is not a symptom in isolation — it is a reassertion of the one thing the captivity made non-negotiable. The cost is enormous and she knows it; she pays it anyway because the alternative — being authored by someone else — is, in her felt experience, indistinguishable from being dead.

The countervailing thread, slower and later to emerge, is **the reparative drive**: the use of her competence and her unspent capacity for love to deliver to others (clients, missing girls, her daughter, the occasional stray who attaches to her office) the specific protections that were not delivered to her. This drive is what saves her from collapse into pure cynicism. It is also what keeps pulling her back into the cape-adjacent world she claims to despise. The dossier should read the contempt as ritual; the actions are the data.

---

## The Core Wound and Its Maintenance

The core wound is **not** the loss of her family, though that is the substrate. It is **not** the powers, though those are the medium. It is the eight months with Killgrave — specifically, the part of those eight months in which she was made to *want* what was done to her and to others. Physical violation she could have integrated as something done *to* a self that remained intact; what was done instead was the demonstration that her self could be reached *inside* and turned. That is the wound: the proof of porousness. Everything since is the construction of a self designed to be impermeable.

The wound is maintained, daily, by four mechanisms:

1. **Phobic encapsulation of the pre-trauma self.** The Jewel costume, the Jewel name, the bright primary-color hero aesthetic, the chosen-by-a-teenager wardrobe — all of it has been quarantined behind a wall of self-mockery so total that no one is permitted to approach it sincerely, including herself. To touch Jewel is to touch the girl Killgrave found and took, and the architecture will not permit that.
2. **Chemical suppression.** Alcohol functions as the affective regulator that her nervous system can no longer provide endogenously. It is not recreational. It is load-bearing. Each drink is a small, deliberate downregulation of a hyperarousal system that would otherwise produce panic, intrusive imagery, or the freeze-then-rage cascade.
3. **Preemptive rejection.** She leaves before she can be left, criticizes before she can be criticized, mocks her own work before anyone else can, sleeps with people she dislikes so that the sexual encounter cannot be a site of meaningful vulnerability. Each preemption confirms the underlying schema (*they would have left / mocked / hurt me*) and so re-installs the wound for another day.
4. **Operational solitude.** The PI practice, the one-person agency, the office she sleeps in, the cases she works without backup — these are not lifestyle preferences. They are the architecture of a person who has concluded that the moment she relies on a system, the system will misread her (the Avengers in the hospital) or coerce her (Killgrave, registration, every authority figure since). Solitude is the structural guarantee against the recurrence of the original mis-recognition.

These four mechanisms are mutually reinforcing and stable. They will not yield to insight alone. They have, over two decades of observation, yielded incrementally and only to the cumulative weight of specific, patient, unforced presence from a small number of people (Cage primarily; the receptionist Malcolm secondarily; Danielle constitutively) who have demonstrated over years that they will neither leave nor try to author her.

---

## Who She Is at the Deepest Level

Beneath the sarcasm, the leather jacket, the bourbon, the profanity, and the file cabinet full of cheating-spouse cases is **a person of unusual moral seriousness who is privately certain she is contaminated and publicly certain that the certainty doesn't matter because the work still has to be done**. The two convictions coexist without contradiction in her interior because she has stopped requiring her self-image to be coherent or flattering. She does not need to be a good person in her own eyes; she needs only to behave, in any given hour, in a way that the contaminated self would not actively be ashamed of by morning. That low bar, scrupulously honored, has produced over years a behavioral record that any outside observer would call ethically excellent.

She is, at depth, **a person who loves more than she will admit** — fiercely, narrowly, and with terror at the implication of each attachment. The love is the most dangerous thing she carries, because it constitutes the precise vector by which she can be reached and hurt. She has not solved this problem. She has accepted the exposure on a case-by-case basis: Cage, Danielle, Malcolm, Carol (recovered, with damage), a handful of clients who became more. Each is a small voluntary porosity in the wall.

She is **a person whose intelligence outruns her self-knowledge**. Her pattern-matching on others is exceptional; her pattern-matching on herself is poor and routinely sabotaged by shame. She will correctly diagnose a client's marriage in twenty minutes and remain incapable of describing her own to herself in twenty years.

She is **a person who has, against the structural odds, refused to become her captor's mirror image**. Killgrave authored people. Jessica refuses to author anyone, including her daughter, including her husband, including her employees. The asymmetry is the moral content of her recovery. It is also the reason she works alone: command and delegation are, in her felt experience, adjacent to coercion, and she will not stand on that ground.

---

## Operational Summary

**Baseline state.** Functional, irritable, mildly intoxicated by mid-afternoon, working. Sleep deficit chronic. Affect flat-to-acerbic. Productivity high in narrow channels (case work, child care), low in everything else (administration, self-care, communication with people who love her).

**Predictable behaviors.** Will take a case if a vulnerable party is involved, even unpaid, even at personal cost. Will refuse a case framed as glamorous, public, or capes-adjacent unless the underlying victim is real. Will lie to her spouse to spare him exposure to a danger she has elected to absorb alone — this is her single most predictable tactical error and the most reliable lever against her. Will not lie to her daughter. Will not strike a non-combatant. Will drink before any encounter she anticipates as emotionally costly. Will arrive late, leave early, sleep on the office couch, return calls in clusters at 2 a.m.

**Trigger inventory (decompensation risks, in descending severity).** Voice-based compulsion or any auditory cue resembling Killgrave's cadence. The color purple deployed deliberately or in concentration. Sexual coercion of any third party in her presence. Threats to Danielle, immediate or implied. Being publicly mis-identified as a villain or as having acted in bad faith, particularly by people whose judgment she respects. Forced sobriety without substitute regulation. Sincere extended praise.

**Stabilizers (de-escalation assets).** Cage's physical presence (specifically presence, not words). Danielle in her sight line. Work to do. Malcolm in the outer office. Alcohol within reach even if not consumed. Solitude on her own terms. A clearly identified predator to move against.

**Counter-manipulation profile.** Resistant to flattery, authority, institutional pressure, sexual leverage, social shaming, and money. Vulnerable to: harm or threatened harm to her attachments; framings that mirror the original Killgrave wound (being unable to explain herself, being mistaken for the perpetrator she is in fact opposing); requests from frightened women or children, which she will honor against her own interests.

**Trajectory.** Slowly improving over the longitudinal record, non-linearly, with relapses keyed to specific stressors rather than to time. The improvement is not therapeutic in origin; it is relational and reparative — the cumulative effect of a marriage, a child, a chosen brother in Malcolm, and a body of work that has, case by case, given her evidence that the contaminated self is also a useful self. She will likely never present as well. She will likely continue to function, and to protect, and to refuse help, and to drink, and to love narrowly and well, for as long as the file remains open.

**Bottom line.** A survivor at work. Treat as such. Do not handle. Do not author. Do not approach the wound directly. Stand where she can see you, stay longer than she expects, and let the evidence accumulate.


## Chronology I — Origin & Childhood

# CHRONOLOGY I — ORIGIN & CHILDHOOD

*Subject: Jessica Campbell (later Jones). Period covered: birth through approximately age 15 (pre-accident). Sources: Alias #22–24 flashbacks; Alias #25–28 ("Purple") flashbacks; The Pulse #5, #11; Jessica Jones (2016) #6 flashback panels; incidental backmatter in Daredevil and Power Man and Iron Fist; cross-reference with Amazing Spider-Man flashback in Alias #16 (Midtown High football game).*

---

## 1.1 Birth, Family of Origin, and Demographic Baseline

Subject was born **Jessica Campbell** to **Brian Campbell** and **Alisa Campbell** (née unspecified in canon), white, of unremarked but presumptively Northern European ancestry, in the New York metropolitan area in the early-to-mid 1980s by floating Marvel chronology (Bendis fixes her age relative to Peter Parker as classmate, *Alias* #16). No canonical hospital of birth; no canonical exact date — a notable absence given the granularity of her later self-narration, and one she herself avoids: across *Alias* she is asked her birthday twice (by Cage, by a clerk) and deflects both times. The deflection is observed and logged: birthdays signify family-of-origin rituals that no longer have practitioners.

**Father — Brian Campbell.** Engineer; specific employer never named. The family's economic profile (Forest Hills detached house, a working family car large enough for a road trip, a son in extracurriculars) suggests a comfortable but unspectacular middle-class household, consistent with mid-career engineering income. Brian appears in three flashback panels across *Alias* and *Pulse*; he is depicted as soft-spoken, glasses-wearing, conflict-averse in domestic micro-scenes (lets Jessica's mother manage the children's disputes). Jessica's residual affect toward him is the most uncomplicated love she expresses anywhere in the corpus — a single panel in *Alias* #22 of him handing her a Walkman is rendered by Gaydos with more facial tenderness than she shows for any adult in present-tense narrative.

**Mother — Alisa Campbell.** Homemaker per *Alias* flashbacks. Depicted as the operational center of the household — managing schedules, vacations, sibling disputes. Subject's relationship to her was the more friction-bearing of the two: typical adolescent-daughter chafe (Jessica eye-rolling at being called to dinner; Alisa noted in *Alias* #22 telling Jessica she's "in one of her moods"). Crucially, the friction was **ordinary** — there is no evidence in canon of abuse, neglect, or even atypical parental harshness. The baseline household was high-functioning. This matters diagnostically: Jessica did not arrive at the chemical-spill accident with a pre-existing attachment injury. Whatever was already irritable in her was temperament, not maltreatment.

**Brother — Phillip Campbell.** Younger by an unstated but apparently small interval (he is rendered as ten-to-twelve in the final road-trip flashback of *Alias* #22 against Jessica's ~fifteen). The sibling dynamic is sketched as low-stakes hostile-affectionate: backseat poking, fights over the Walkman in the accident sequence ("give it back, you little — "), the universal grammar of siblings. Phillip is the family member Jessica references least in adult life, and the omission is itself a finding: he is the loss that cannot be metabolized into the romantic-tragic frame of "lost parents," because losing a younger sibling carries the additional load of having been, by birth order, his protector.

## 1.2 Geography, Domicile, and Daily Environment

The Campbells lived in **Forest Hills, Queens** — established by Jessica's attendance at the same high school as Peter Parker (canonically Midtown High in this era of Bendis's continuity; *Alias* #16 places her in the bleachers at a Midtown football game where Parker is humiliated). Forest Hills in this period: solidly middle-class, tree-lined, predominantly single-family detached homes, a long subway ride from Manhattan, culturally Queens-Jewish-and-Catholic-mixed. The Campbell home is depicted only in fragments — a kitchen with patterned wallpaper (*Alias* #22), a teenage bedroom with band posters (Pulse #5), a driveway with a station wagon (*Alias* #22). These are visual ciphers for "ordinary American family circa late 90s," and they are rendered, by Gaydos, with the deliberate flatness of memory that has been worn smooth.

A total-surveillance file would log the following inferable daily geography of childhood: the bus stop or walk to elementary school (PS unspecified, but Forest Hills PS 144 or 196 by neighborhood plausibility); the local pizza place; the synagogue or church (unestablished — no canonical religion of family, and Jessica's adult profanity is unstructured by sectarian patterning, which weakly suggests secular or lapsed); the route home past Peter Parker's house (canonically established as Forest Hills, two streets away — and the proximity matters; see §1.5).

## 1.3 Early Behavioral Substrate (Pre-Accident Temperament)

This is the most under-attended period in the public record and the most diagnostically important. From the scattered flashback evidence, a coherent pre-trauma temperament can be reconstructed:

- **Irritable-dysphoric baseline.** Multiple flashbacks (*Alias* #22, #24, #28; Pulse #5) depict adolescent Jessica with the same facial set she carries into adulthood: brow slightly drawn, mouth slightly tight, gaze slightly down-and-left. Gaydos draws her this way deliberately — adolescent Jessica is a smaller, less battered version of adult Jessica, not a sunnier one. This is consistent with a constitutional dysthymic-leaning temperament that the trauma would later amplify but did not create.

- **Compare-and-resent cognitive habit.** Her flashback monologue about Peter Parker in *Alias* #16 is the cardinal exhibit. She did not know he was Spider-Man. She knew him only as a sad, picked-on Midtown nerd, and even him she resented — not for being better than her, but for occupying a legible social position (the bullied smart kid) while she occupied none. The habit of measuring herself against contemporaries and finding the comparison intolerable is established **before** powers, before Killgrave, before everything. It is foundational.

- **Privacy-seeking, observation-oriented.** She is described and drawn as a watcher, not a participant — at the football game she is in the stands alone; in the kitchen flashback she reads while her mother talks; in the car she has the Walkman on. The investigator she becomes is already present in the schoolgirl who would rather observe than transact.

- **Sullen-but-not-acting-out.** No canonical reference to delinquency, school discipline, running away, early substance use, or sexual precocity. Her adolescence was internally turbulent and externally compliant — a profile that often produces high-functioning adults who collapse when the external scaffolding fails.

- **Verbal precocity, masked.** Her adult fluency, vocabulary, and reading speed (she reads case files at a clip Cage notes with amusement in *New Avengers*) imply a literate childhood. No depicted intellectual mentor, no scene of a teacher singling her out, but the equipment is built somewhere, and the household — engineer father, homemaker mother of evident competence — is the plausible site.

- **No early friendships of record.** This is the negative-space finding. Across the entire corpus, Jessica never references a childhood friend by name. Not one. There is no "I knew her since we were eight." There is Peter Parker, whom she watched from across a cafeteria and never spoke to. The implication is either (a) friendships existed and were severed/forgotten as part of the post-accident scorched-earth, or (b) she was an isolate well before the accident. The evidence weakly favors (b): the adult who has zero retained childhood-friend infrastructure usually did not have it to begin with.

## 1.4 The Family Microclimate

Surveillance-grade reconstruction of household rhythms, from canonical fragments and reasonable inference:

- **Meals taken together** (kitchen flashback in *Alias* #22 implies family dinner as default; mother's call to dinner is rendered as routine, not novel).
- **Family vacations as institution** — the road trip in the accident sequence was clearly framed as a familiar ritual, not a one-off; the packed station wagon, the assigned seats, the sibling bickering, all have the texture of repetition. Disney World was the destination (*Alias* #22) — a middle-American family-vacation cliché that Jessica's adult sensibility would mock but that her child self had loved enough to be excited about, per the flashback dialogue.
- **Father as quiet center, mother as operational center.** A common 1990s middle-class division of labor. Jessica's adult difficulty being "handled" or "managed" by women in particular (her snapping at Carol Danvers's directive style across *New Avengers*) may track distally to this template.
- **Phillip-Jessica dyad as the children's subunit.** Their squabbles were the household's background noise. The sudden, total absence of that noise, in the months after the accident, is one of the unspoken textures of the Jones-household adoption years.

There is no depicted extended family — no grandparents, no aunts, no uncles, no cousins ever invoked in any storyline. If they existed, they did not present themselves to claim Jessica after the accident (the Jones family adoption proceeded without contested custody). If they did not exist, the Campbells were already a nuclear-only unit, which intensifies the catastrophe: the accident did not just kill her family, it killed her **entire family system**.

## 1.5 The Parker Adjacency

Worth a subsection because of how much weight Bendis places on it in *Alias* #16. Subject attended Midtown High during the years Peter Parker did. She did not know him. She watched him. She resented him — not for anything he had done, but for the fact that her noticing of him meant she had noticed someone else's life as more dimensionally available than her own. In her flashback monologue she narrates that she had a brief, half-formed wish to do something kind for him (the football-game scene, where he is humiliated); she did not. The non-action is the data point. Adolescent Jessica had compassionate impulses and a paralysis that prevented their expression. Adult Jessica's hardest-won achievement will be the inverse: acting on those impulses despite the paralysis (taking cases, taking in Malcolm, taking the baby). The Parker memory is the template she is rewriting for the rest of her life. That she is rewriting Peter Parker's bleachers, of all things, is the kind of irony she would never permit herself to articulate.

## 1.6 Pre-Existing Capacities That Will Matter Later

Cataloguing the equipment she brought to the trauma, because the equipment partly determines the trauma's outcome:

- **A securely-attached early childhood**, sufficient to lay down an internal working model of "people can be trusted" that, though savaged, was not eradicated. This is why she can eventually love Cage and mother Danielle. Adults without this substrate generally cannot.
- **A moral floor laid down by parents who modeled ordinary decency.** No canonical scene of Brian or Alisa instructing her ethically, but the floor's existence in the adult is evidence of its installation in the child.
- **Habits of observation and verbal articulation** that will become the operational substrate of Alias Investigations.
- **A dysphoric-irritable temperament** that will become both her armor (resistant to manipulation by flattery) and her vulnerability (resistant to receiving care).
- **Isolate tendencies** that will become both her tactical strength (she can work alone for long periods without decompensating from loneliness alone) and her structural weakness (she does not know how to ask).

## 1.7 The Final Pre-Trauma Frame

The last canonical image of the intact Campbell family (*Alias* #22): the station wagon on a highway, Phillip in the backseat with Jessica, Walkman dispute in progress, parents in front, Disney-bound. Brian is glancing at the rear-view; Alisa is half-turned. The military convoy is not yet on the page. This is the photograph the file keeps at the top of the folder. Everything after — the months of coma, the waking into a different family, the powers she will hide, the costume she will later loathe, the captivity, the bottle, the office couch, the daughter — is downstream of the next panel.

The child in that car is not yet the woman in the file. But she is already, demonstrably: watchful, irritable, lonely in ways she has not named, loved in ways she has not yet had to lose, and equipped — quietly, by the unspectacular competence of two ordinary parents — with the moral and cognitive infrastructure that will, against everything done to it, refuse to break.

*End Chronology I. Continues: Chronology II — The Accident, the Coma, and the Jones Household.*


## Chronology II — Formation & Emergence

# CHRONOLOGY II — FORMATION & EMERGENCE

*Subject: Jessica Campbell Jones (DOB inferred late 1970s, Forest Hills, Queens, NY). Period covered: approximately age 13 through age 22 — from the months immediately preceding the Route 4 collision through the dissolution of the "Jewel" identity and the founding act of withdrawal that becomes Alias Investigations. All times Eastern. All addresses Queens unless otherwise specified.*

---

## 1. THE PRE-COLLISION SELF (Ages 13–15.5)

### 1.1 Household structure and the engineering father

The Campbell residence — a two-story colonial with a detached garage in the middle-middle stratum of Forest Hills — operated on what canonical flashbacks consistently render as a slightly stifling routine. Father Brian Campbell, engineer, kept the household clock: dinner at a fixed hour, weekend errands queued, the family car (a sedan, dark color, repeatedly shown in flashback panels with the rear deck packed for vacation) maintained obsessively. He is depicted as the affectionate but distracted parent — the one who *organized* the road trip rather than the one who *wanted* it. Mother is the emotional weather of the house and the principal antagonist of adolescent Jessica's daily life: the homework enforcer, the curfew-keeper, the voice cutting through the car on the day of the accident telling Jessica to put away the Walkman.

The last family conversation captured in flashback (recurring across *Alias* and *Jessica Jones* 2016) is a banal argument: mother riding her about her grades and her attitude, Jessica sulking with headphones in, brother Phillip in the back seat being the easy child. Jessica's last words to her mother, per the canonical re-staging she revisits in dreams, are a snapped *"Shut up — God."* This single sentence becomes the prosecutor in her interior court for the next decade.

### 1.2 The brother

Phillip is younger by roughly three to four years; old enough to be a witness and a rival, young enough that Jessica's adolescent self treats him with the contemptuous custodianship of an oldest child. The file should note that Jessica's later behavior with children — with Danielle most of all, but also with Mattie Franklin, with the runaways and missing kids that recur as Alias case material — has a precise template in the brother she failed (in her interior accounting) to protect. The reparative impulse is brother-shaped before it is daughter-shaped.

### 1.3 Midtown High and the Parker fixation

Subject attended Midtown High School in Forest Hills; the campus is the same locus as Peter Parker's. The Parker fixation, dramatized in *Alias* #22's flashback issue, is granular and worth recording in surveillance detail because it pre-dates the powers and predicts the post-power resentment of capes:

- She *noticed* Parker before anyone had reason to. Not romantically — diagnostically. She clocked him as a target of bullies (Flash Thompson's circle) and as inexplicably uninteresting to the social order. She was, in her own retrospective telling, irritated by him: by his passivity, by his apparent contentment with low status, by the unaccountable sense she had that there was something *more* to him she could not name.
- She wrote, briefly and abortively, what she now describes with maximum self-flagellation as a "poem" about him. The artifact is never shown; its existence is canon. She has never recanted the embarrassment.
- The crush, such as it was, was structured around a yearning for someone who saw the social hierarchy the way she did — from outside and below — and who might validate her resentment.

The Parker datum is the cleanest pre-trauma window into her temperament: she was a watcher, not a participant; she catalogued people; she developed unspoken theories about them; she preferred private contempt to public engagement. The PI was already in the schoolgirl.

### 1.4 Adolescent affect baseline

Multiple flashbacks confirm a baseline that is best characterized as **irritable dysphoric with social envy.** She compared herself unfavorably to popular girls, sneered at the comparison even as she made it, fought with her mother about clothing and grades, kept her bedroom door closed, and had — by the evidence — no close confidante. There is no canonical best friend from the pre-collision period. The absence is itself the data: she enters her trauma without a peer infrastructure to fall back on.

This baseline matters because it forecloses the consoling fiction that Jessica was a *bright* girl ruined by tragedy. She was a sullen, intelligent, isolated girl made catastrophically more so. Whatever sweetness later writers grant her in flashback is bracketed by her own corrosive, accurate self-mockery.

---

## 2. THE COLLISION (Approximate age 15.5)

### 2.1 Route, timing, mechanism

The Campbell family was returning from a Disney World vacation — a fact Jessica weaponizes against herself permanently afterward, the bathos of being orphaned coming home from *the happiest place on Earth.* The collision occurred on a divided highway. A U.S. Army convoy carrying experimental chemical materials lost control of a lead vehicle; a tanker overturned into the oncoming lanes. The Campbell sedan was struck head-on. The convoy is canonically tied, in later retcons, to the same military-industrial substrate that produced other irradiated entities — the relevant fact for the dossier is that the chemicals were not standard fuel; they were a classified compound.

### 2.2 The Walkman / the argument

In the seconds before impact, Jessica had headphones on and was mid-argument with her mother. She does not remember the impact. She remembers — and re-remembers, in nightmare loop — the argument, the song that was playing (canonically left vague, but described as something she now cannot listen to), and the specific irritation in her chest at her mother's voice. The interior conviction that *she was being a bad daughter at the exact moment her family died* is the substrate of her shame architecture before Killgrave is ever introduced.

### 2.3 Casualty distribution

Brian, the mother, and Phillip: killed at the scene or shortly after. Jessica: critical, transported, declared unlikely to survive. Chemical exposure during the wreck — dermal and inhalational, doused — is the vector. She was the only family member proximate to the breached tank in a way that produced absorption.

### 2.4 Coma

Approximate duration: six months. The file should treat this coma as the **first dissociative episode of her life** and the somatic template for every later "checking out" under stress. She lost half a school year, missed her own family's funerals, and woke into a world where her name was attached to a single survivor of a four-person family — a status she had no rehearsal for.

The Joneses — adoptive parents whose surname she retains — appear during or shortly after the coma. The relationship to them is canonically thin. They are decent people. She resents them for being decent and not dead. She moves into their household, finishes high school under their roof, and refuses, with the slow consistency of an adolescent in mourning who has no language for it, to call them anything that would imply she had let her real family go.

---

## 3. POWER ONSET AND THE YEARS OF CONCEALMENT (Ages ~16–18)

### 3.1 First manifestations

Powers emerge gradually post-coma. The canonical sequence:

- **Strength first.** Discovered in a non-dramatic domestic context — a door handle she snapped, a piece of furniture she moved one-handed and then sat down beside, dizzy. She did not tell the Joneses. She tested it in private, in the basement and the backyard, on inanimate objects.
- **Durability second.** Inferred from injuries that healed faster than expected and impacts that didn't bruise her. She does not test this deliberately; it is observational.
- **Flight third — and badly.** The latest and most psychologically disturbing capacity. She refers to it her whole adult life as *"controlled falling,"* a description that is at once technically accurate (she has limited active control and tends to descend) and a damning self-assessment. The flight onset is depicted as humiliating: unbidden, uncoordinated, often producing rough landings. She loathes the body sensation of it.

### 3.2 The concealment as identity-forming

She told **no one** about the powers for an extended interval — at minimum a year, plausibly longer. The concealment is not strategic; it is shame-coded. Powers were the thing she had instead of a family. To reveal them would be to make the trade explicit and to invite the world to evaluate whether she had received fair compensation. (She had not.)

The concealment period is also the period in which she begins:
- Sleeping irregularly.
- Drinking — first the Joneses' liquor cabinet, then her own supply, beer mostly at this stage.
- Skipping school and getting away with it because grief produces institutional tolerance.
- Reading the city. She walks Queens and Manhattan at night during this period, alone, in a manner that prefigures her PI fieldcraft: she learns the geography of fire escapes, of after-hours diners, of where police don't go.

### 3.3 The Parker disclosure (oblique)

In flashback she encounters Parker, post-power, in a stairwell or hallway moment that approximates a confession she cannot make. He is by this point Spider-Man, though she does not know it. He is kind to her in the brief, awkward way teenagers are kind across grief gradients. She does not tell him. The Parker fixation transmutes here from envy to a more painful sense of parallel — *something happened to him too, didn't it* — without ever resolving into contact. This is the first instance of a behavioral pattern that becomes permanent: she identifies kinship and then declines to claim it.

---

## 4. THE JEWEL EMERGENCE (Approximately ages 18–20)

### 4.1 The decision to suit up

The Jewel period is canonically brief — months to perhaps two years of active hero work — but it occupies a vastly disproportionate share of her interior real estate because it represents her **only attempt at sincere participation in the moral economy of capes.** The motivating logic, reconstructed from inference and flashback:

- She has powers and no family. Powers without use are an accusation.
- The cultural ambient (Avengers, Fantastic Four, the late-90s Marvel hero landscape) frames superpowers as a vocation.
- She wants — and this is the datum she most punishes herself for later — to be *seen.* Not as the orphan. As someone whose powers compensate for, even out, the cosmic injustice done to her.

### 4.2 The costume and the name

The costume is the artifact of maximum shame in her entire history, more even than the post-Killgrave artifacts. Canonical description: pale, sparkly, midriff or short-sleeved, with the kind of bright color palette she now reads as humiliating; she is on record describing it in language she would not use of her worst enemies. The name "Jewel" was, per flashback, **not chosen by her.** Carol Danvers helped pick it — a fact Jessica will not forgive Carol for in any operationally meaningful way, even though their adult friendship survives it. The name's diminutive, decorative, ornamental connotation is precisely calibrated to the wound: she was given a name that meant *pretty thing*, and she wore it.

Important behavioral inference: the Jewel-shame is not really about aesthetics. The aesthetic shame is a decoy. What she cannot bear is that she was, briefly, *sincere.* She believed in the project. She wanted in. The costume is the evidence of that earnestness, and the costume is what Killgrave found her wearing.

### 4.3 Operational Jewel — what she actually did

Bendis declines to over-document the Jewel cases; the file should record this as authorially deliberate — Jewel is a memory, not a chronicle. What we can establish:

- She operated street-level, in New York, without team affiliation but with peripheral contact with established heroes (the Carol friendship dates from here; she met Ms. Marvel as a hero and was, briefly, a younger-sister figure to her).
- She was on cordial-to-warm terms with at least one Avengers-adjacent figure (Carol) and was on a trajectory toward Avengers consideration — never formalized.
- She was photographed publicly enough that the costume exists in newspaper morgues and that Killgrave could identify her by sight.
- She fought, per implication, the standard ladder of low-tier New York antagonists. No defining heroic victory is on record. No defining failure either, until the one.

### 4.4 The pre-Killgrave equilibrium

The crucial datum about the months immediately preceding the abduction is that **she was, by her own retrospective standard, the closest to happy she has been as an adult.** She had a vocation, a friend in Carol, an apartment (likely shared, possibly studio in Manhattan; canonical detail thin), a public identity she had not yet learned to hate, and a body that was beginning to feel like hers. The Killgrave abduction is therefore not a wound on a wounded subject — it is a wound on the brief plateau of her one attempt at recovery. The total destruction of that plateau is what makes the wound irrecoverable in the form the original self would have recognized.

---

## 5. THE KILLGRAVE CAPTIVITY (~Eight months, age ~20)

### 5.1 The acquisition

She was identified, located, and taken in costume. Canonical flashbacks place the initial encounter in a restaurant — she walked in on a Killgrave atrocity in progress; he turned to her, registered the costume and the strength capacity, and *kept her.* The acquisition is depicted in *Alias #25* with deliberate sparseness: she does not get to fight. She gets a sentence. The sentence rewrites her.

### 5.2 The captivity architecture

Eight months is the canonical figure. The conditions, reconstructed:

- **No physical restraints.** She was capable, at any moment, of physically dismantling him. The cage was linguistic. He instructed her not to leave; she did not leave.
- **Domestic proximity, not dungeon.** He kept her in hotel suites, mansions, restaurants. She ate at his table. She was *present* in his social field. He paraded her.
- **Sexual coercion without rape.** The canonical clarification — repeated by the author because the distinction matters — is that he never physically penetrated her. What he did was force her to *want him to.* He instructed her to beg. He instructed her to stand naked for hours. He made her watch him with other women and instructed her to wish she were them. He withheld the act she had been compelled to crave. The cruelty of this specific structure is its destruction of the inner sanctum: he didn't violate her body, he violated the part of her that decides what her body wants. The file should record this as the operational definition of her central wound: **not the loss of bodily safety, but the loss of authorship over her own desire.**
- **Coerced witnessing of violence against others.** She watched him compel violence and humiliation in others while she stood by, also compelled. This is the layer of survivor's guilt overlaid on the consent-wound: she was present at others' suffering and *participated by inaction.*
- **Coerced attacks.** He used her, intermittently, as muscle. She hurt people on his instruction. She cannot enumerate them all.

### 5.3 The eight-month interior

What did she do with her own mind in eight months of compelled obedience? The canonical answer is fragmentary but consistent: she dissociated. She found interior rooms he had not specifically forbidden her to occupy. She *hated* in private — and the hate was, paradoxically, the part of her he could not reach, because he did not think to forbid it. This is the seed of her adult operational code: **silent contempt as the last preserve of the self under coercion.** It is why she defaults to it later under far lesser pressures. It worked once when nothing else could.

She also, throughout, missed her dead family — and the missing was the second preserve. He could not compel her to forget them. She rehearsed them.

### 5.4 The Avengers attack and the second coma

Killgrave eventually instructed her to kill the Avengers. She found the Mansion. She misidentified the Scarlet Witch — the canonical detail is that she attacked the wrong red-and-yellow woman, hitting Carol Danvers / Ms. Marvel, who was her *friend* — and the Avengers, not knowing she was compelled, fought back. Vision and Iron Man brought her down. Beating severe enough to induce coma. Second extended dissociative-from-body episode of her young life.

The Avengers' failure of recognition is the operational hinge of the rest of her psychology and must be documented as such. **No one came to the hospital.** Carol — her friend — did not visit, because Carol, like everyone else, believed she had gone villain. The hospitalization was prolonged. She lay unattended in a public ward until the X-Men, with Jean Grey, intervened; Jean woke her and installed a psychic block against Killgrave's compulsion. The block is the single most important piece of psychiatric infrastructure she carries; she is unaware of it for years.

The non-visit is the wound under the wound. Killgrave took her will; the Avengers took her standing. She wakes alone, in a costume she now hates, with the knowledge that the people she thought were her colleagues believed she had defected. The shame of the Jewel costume and the shame of the Avenger-hospital silence fuse here into a single permanent texture.

---

## 6. THE INTERREGNUM AND THE KNIGHTRESS ATTEMPT (Approximately age 20–21)

### 6.1 Discharge into nothing

She leaves the hospital with no support structure. The Joneses are not depicted as present. Carol is not present. She has no apartment to return to in any sense that matters. She finds rooms, drinks, sleeps badly. The drinking, which had been an adolescent habit and a Jewel-era social activity, becomes a load-bearing structural element of daily life in this period. The shift from *drinking* to *alcoholism* should be dated here.

### 6.2 The Knightress experiment

She tries again — once — to be in costume, and this time she designs the costume herself: dark, armored-looking, hostile, with a mask. The name **Knightress** is self-chosen and is the precise inverse of "Jewel" — armored where Jewel was decorative, anonymous where Jewel was photogenic, punitive in tone where Jewel was reassuring. The Knightress period is even shorter than Jewel and is more punitive in its operational style: she fights harder, hurts opponents more, and does not pretend to enjoy it.

Knightress is interrupted by the rooftop encounter with Luke Cage. The canonical setup: she had pursued a small-time villain (a woman with a child in tow, which she would not have done if she had known about the child; the child-detail snags her), and Cage was working a related angle. They fight side-by-side, leave the child safe, and end the evening at a bar.

### 6.3 The first Cage encounter

They have sex that night. The canonical depiction is mutual, uncomplicated, and unmemorable to neither of them; she leaves in the morning without naming herself. Cage knows her only as Knightress, and only briefly. The encounter is significant for what it *does not* produce: she does not stay; she does not return; she does not reach out. She has just learned that intimacy with a powerful other is dangerous, and the lesson is fresh enough that she cannot tolerate the inverse experience — kindness from a powerful other — without fleeing it.

This is the first iteration of what will become a permanent pattern: **she sleeps with Luke Cage when she cannot bear what is happening to her, and she does not stay.** The pattern recurs in *Alias #1*.

### 6.4 The decision to quit

There is no canonical single moment of decision. The Knightress identity simply stops appearing. The costume goes into a closet or a dumpster (canonically ambiguous; she does not refer to it again). She makes the underlying determination that she will not wear another costume, will not take another hero-name, will not present herself for evaluation by capes or by the public as a *person who fights crime in costume.*

The negative space of this decision is the founding act of her adult identity. Everything that follows — the office, the name *Alias*, the leather jacket, the profanity, the refusal of Avengers invitations she will receive for years — is the carrying-out of this single, never-articulated vow.

---

## 7. THE FOUNDING OF ALIAS (Age ~22)

### 7.1 The office

Address: a low-rent walkup in Manhattan, modest square footage, single room plus a small bathroom, a couch she sleeps on more often than is healthy, a desk facing the door, a filing cabinet, a coffeemaker, a window onto a fire escape. The door bears the legend **ALIAS INVESTIGATIONS** in lettering she did not pay extra for. The name is a multi-layered private joke and confession: an alias is a false name (she has had two and discarded both); an alias is also a *known-as* (the legal-PI convention); and "Alias" as a business identity is the closest she will come to acknowledging that she is operating out of the wreckage of two prior identities without ever naming them.

She lives at the office. The home/work distinction collapses immediately and will not meaningfully re-separate until she moves in with Cage years later. The collapse is functional: she does not have to commute past a domestic space that would remind her she has no domestic life.

### 7.2 Licensing and the institutional posture

She obtains a PI license — a fact that requires her to interact with the state in a documented way and that establishes her as legitimately working within civil law. This is significant: she could have operated as an unlicensed vigilante; she chose to operate as a licensed citizen. The choice encodes the post-Killgrave imperative to be *accountable on paper* — to have a record that says she works inside the rules, even as she breaks them in fieldwork. The license is also a hedge against being mistaken, again, for a villain. She wants the paperwork to disagree with the assumption.

### 7.3 Case selection and the early casework

The first cases are bottom-of-the-barrel: spousal-fidelity surveillance, missing pets escalating to missing persons, insurance fraud, the small-claims end of the moral universe. She takes them because they pay and because they do not require her to engage with the cape world. She runs them competently. She drinks through them. She develops the operational habits that the dossier will record in later sections — the long stationary surveillance, the camera in the bag, the willingness to sit in a car for nine hours, the use of strength only as a last resort and never on camera.

The cape world refuses to let her stay quit. The first major Alias case (the Captain America identity / political conspiracy arc of *Alias* #1–9) drags her immediately back into the orbit of the powered. She handles it. She is shaken by it. She does not change her business model. The pattern of *being pulled back to the world she is hiding from, refusing to let the pull become a return* is the structural rhythm of her entire PI career, and it is set in the first arc.

### 7.4 The drinking, codified

By this period the drinking is daily, structural, and operationally factored. Morning coffee with a finger of something. Beer through the afternoon if the case allows. Whiskey at night. The bar of choice is Josie's — a Hell's Kitchen dive she shares with the broader Daredevil-adjacent set, though she does not socialize with them; she drinks alone in their proximity. The drinking accomplishes three things she cannot accomplish otherwise: it dampens the nightmare-volume, it slows the startle reflex, and it gives the days an organizing rhythm in the absence of any other.

### 7.5 The sexual self-administration

In the period between the Knightress encounter and the *Alias #1* re-encounter with Cage, she has occasional, joyless sexual contacts — a pattern depicted as self-medicating and dissociative, not pleasure-seeking. The encounters end with her unable or unwilling to


## Chronology III — The Defining Middle Period

# CHRONOLOGY III — THE DEFINING MIDDLE PERIOD

*Approximate operational window: opening of Alias Investigations through the dissolution of the New Avengers and the close of the Bendis Avengers continuity — roughly a decade of subject-time. This is the longest continuous behavioral record we possess and the period in which Subject's adult operating system stabilized into the form it still wears.*

---

## 1. The Office on the Lower Floor: Establishment of Alias Investigations

The middle period opens with Subject in a two-room walk-up office in a non-prestige building (street-level entrance flanked by a bodega and a check-cashing storefront in the early issues; the building's other tenants are a chiropractor of uncertain license and an accountant). The office is a deliberate downgrade — Subject could afford better, has demonstrably earned more in single cases than her annual rent, and chooses this space for what it screens out. Walk-in clientele self-selects to the desperate, the cheap, and the embarrassed; the cape community will not be caught dead on this stairwell. This is the architectural form of her shame management: a workplace that publicly announces she is not a hero.

**Spatial inventory (steady state through the period):** wooden desk with a chair facing the door (defensible sightline), a sagging couch she sleeps on more nights than she sleeps in her apartment, a dorm refrigerator stocked predominantly with beer and condiments, an answering machine she lets fill, a filing cabinet whose drawers correspond loosely to year and not at all to case, a frosted-glass door with the agency name in sign-painter's lettering she paid extra for in a rare flash of professional vanity. No diplomas, no licenses framed (she has the PI license — it lives in a drawer). No photographs displayed. The window faces an airshaft. The bathroom is shared with the hall.

**Operational pattern.** Cases come in three streams: walk-in (cheating spouse, deadbeat parent, low-grade insurance fraud); referral from a slowly accreting network of attorneys and bail bondsmen who learn she will take the cases other PIs balk at; and the involuntary stream — the cape-adjacent cases that her reputation magnetizes whether she wants them or not. She charges a sliding scale she will not admit she charges, often comping the desperate and overbilling the wealthy by a factor sufficient to subsidize the comps. Bookkeeping is performative; she has no accountant and pays taxes late, every year, with a self-prepared return that triggers no audit because the numbers are unambitious enough to bore the IRS.

**Daily rhythm in this period.** Wakes between 10 and noon, generally on the office couch or fully clothed on top of her bed at the apartment. First coffee from the bodega downstairs (regular, two sugars — she has not varied the order in years; the counterman stops asking by year two). First beer between 2 and 4 PM if no fieldwork is scheduled, deferred until evening if it is. Fieldwork — surveillance, follow, doorstep — concentrated in late afternoon and evening, when subjects are mobile and lighting favors a watcher. Eats irregularly: bodega sandwiches, takeout Chinese from one of three rotating places, the occasional diner breakfast at 3 AM after a long sit. Returns to office or apartment after midnight. Drinks until sleep. Repeats.

## 2. The Cases as Behavioral Spine

The investigative casework of the middle period reads, viewed longitudinally, less as discrete files than as a single extended psychological project: each case a controlled re-exposure to a fragment of her own unprocessed material, undertaken under the protective fiction that she is being paid to look at someone else's life.

**The political case (an Avenger's secret identity).** Early in the period a client pays her to confirm a public figure's covert costumed identity; the case spirals into national-security territory and ends with Captain America in her office at dawn explaining why she will not publish. Subject's behavioral takeaways from this file are foundational for the period: (a) she will never again take a case in which a client's framing is the only framing she has; (b) she begins drinking before client meetings rather than only after; (c) she discovers she can be in a room with Captain America without dissolving — a small re-attachment datum she does not let herself notice.

**The Mattie Franklin file.** A teenage Spider-Woman missing, found in a brownstone basement being chemically harvested for Mutant Growth Hormone by men who film what they do to her. Subject's response sequence on entry to the basement is, on close read, the cleanest single behavioral document we have of her core operating mode under acute moral threat: situational assessment in under three seconds, force application calibrated precisely to incapacitation without lethality (a discipline she maintains across the period — she has never killed on panel, and the restraint is volitional, not circumstantial), evacuation of the victim before pursuit of secondary objectives, and — critically — a refusal to call in cape backup that would have been faster and safer. She extracts Mattie alone, drives her to a hospital herself, sits in the waiting room until a guardian arrives, and does not bill the case. The behavioral signature here — *protect the weaker party, refuse the cavalry, eat the cost* — recurs across every subsequent major file in the period.

**The small-town/Captain America impersonator case.** Subject travels to a depressed rural town to investigate a missing teenager and finds herself enmeshed in a fabricated Americana — a man wearing a degraded Captain America costume, a community organized around a lie, sexual violence concealed under a flag. She drinks more heavily on this case than on any other in the corpus, sleeps in her car, and returns to New York visibly altered. The case is structurally a re-staging of her Killgrave material in costume: a charismatic male authority figure coercing a community into participation in his violence. She does not articulate the parallel; her body articulates it for her in the form of three days of post-case bedrest, an unanswered phone, and a binge that ends only when Luke Cage shows up at her door uninvited.

**The Rick Jones identity case.** A man claiming to be Rick Jones hires her to prove he is Rick Jones. The case is comic on its surface and structurally devastating underneath: Subject is being asked to authenticate an identity, the precise cognitive operation Killgrave rendered unsafe in her own life. She handles it with unusual gentleness — does not mock the client, runs the verifications, delivers the answer (he is not), and accepts a kind of friendship out of the case that survives, intermittently, into the later period.

**The Purple Man re-emergence (terminal case of the Alias period).** Killgrave escapes containment and re-enters her field. Behavioral record across this arc: insomnia total, drinking initially elevated then sharply curtailed (she sobers up to fight him — a datum), full withdrawal from Luke Cage during the run-up (she does not tell him), tactical preparation that includes the psychic mantra Jean Grey installed years prior. The confrontation itself is the single most disciplined sustained-affect performance in her record: she stands in front of him, he commands her, the command lands on the psychic firewall, she registers nothing on her face, she takes him apart with her hands. After, she does not celebrate. She goes home, sleeps for sixteen hours, wakes, calls Cage, and asks him to come over. This is, by her standards, an unprecedented act of post-crisis reaching-out.

## 3. The Cage Pivot

The Luke Cage relationship is the central interpersonal achievement of the middle period and warrants extended granular treatment because its mechanics illustrate every adaptive and maladaptive pattern Subject possesses operating at once.

**Pre-history.** A one-night stand during her brief Knightress phase; a second encounter, drunk and shame-laden, in the early Alias period that she cannot face the next morning and that he chooses, with characteristic restraint, not to weaponize. He does not pursue. He does not ghost. He simply remains available. This is the rarest stimulus in her environment — a powerful man whose attention is non-coercive and patient — and her initial response to it is to treat it as suspicious.

**Courtship behavior (her side).** Inversion of conventional pursuit. She insults him reflexively in his presence. She arrives at his apartment late, drunk, without warning, and leaves before morning. She introduces him to no one. She refers to him to others, when forced, as "this guy I'm seeing," with the same vocal flatness she uses for utilities. Concurrently, and without acknowledgment: she begins eating breakfast more often (he cooks). She drinks slightly less on nights she stays at his place. She defers small decisions to him in domains where she does not feel competent (restaurant choice, which movie) and holds the line absolutely in domains where she does (case strategy, what she wears, whether she will attend any function that involves the word "gala"). The asymmetric deference is itself a tell — she is letting him in via the channels her shame does not patrol.

**The pregnancy.** Unplanned. Her recorded first response is twenty-four hours of silent panic, a binge cut short by nausea she misreads as hangover, a missed appointment with a client, and a phone call to Carol Danvers — the first time in the period she initiates contact with Carol since the original Killgrave-era estrangement. The choice to carry to term is made over a span of days, not consultatively; she tells Cage after deciding. He responds with the steady non-coercion that defines him and the relationship; she responds to his response by crying for the first time in front of him, which she experiences as a humiliation and which he correctly does not name.

**Pregnancy somatic record.** Reduced but not eliminated drinking (this is a documentable struggle, not a clean abstinence — there are panels showing her holding a beer she does not drink; there are also implied lapses); cessation of smoking, sustained; cessation of frontline fieldwork by the second trimester at Cage's insistence and her grudging acceptance; transfer of investigative work to phone, computer, and proxy. Sleep improves marginally because she is exhausted; nightmares persist and may worsen. Hypervigilance about fetal harm reaches near-pathological pitch — she runs threat scenarios constantly, has a contingency plan for premature labor under attack conditions, and refuses to be alone in the apartment for the final six weeks, a concession of independence that costs her real psychological currency.

**Childbirth and immediate postpartum.** Hospital birth, Cage present, complications minimal. Danielle Cage is born and Subject's affective baseline reorganizes inside a week. The pre-existing protective drive — already her dominant under-stress motivator — finds its terminal object. From this point forward, every major decision in the record can be modeled as a function of Danielle's safety as a primary variable.

**Marriage.** Civil, small, Matt Murdock officiating in some accounts, attended by a curated short list. Subject wears something she selected herself and complained about for weeks. She does not change her name. The wedding photographs, where they appear in later issues, sit on Cage's bookshelf, not hers.

## 4. The Pulse: Subject Inside an Institution

The Daily Bugle hiring is the period's principal experiment in placing Subject inside a hierarchical institution she does not control. The job is offered partly as a favor, partly as Jameson's calculation that her cape-world access is worth her attitude, and accepted by Subject for two reasons she will not articulate together: she needs the income with a baby coming, and the work — investigative reporting on superhuman affairs — is a structurally legitimized version of the rubbernecking she has already been doing for years.

**Behavioral pattern at the Bugle.** Refuses a desk in the bullpen; works from a corner she annexes. Does not socialize with staff; develops a single working relationship with Ben Urich, whose deliberate manner and ethical seriousness she respects. Conflicts with Jameson are frequent, loud, and structurally identical each time: he attempts to direct her framing, she refuses, he threatens, she calls his bluff, he capitulates because her copy is good. She files clean prose under deadline — a competence the file should not under-record. She breaks the Green Goblin/Norman Osborn re-emergence story under hazardous conditions, weathers professional retaliation, and quits the job on her own terms when Cage's safety and the pregnancy make the calculus untenable.

**Institutional takeaway.** She can function inside an institution she despises if the work is real and the leash is long. She cannot function inside one whose values she must pretend to share. This will recur.

## 5. Avengers Tower: Cohabitation with the Cape World

Following the new team's formation and Cage's recruitment, Subject relocates with the baby into Avengers Tower. This is the most aesthetically and psychologically incongruous living arrangement of her life — the Jewel-shame quarantine breached daily by the elevator opening on Iron Man — and the behavioral record of this cohabitation phase is unusually rich.

**Adaptation pattern.** Refuses Stark-provisioned amenities where refusal is possible (does not use the gym, the medical bay for routine matters, the staff). Accepts where refusal would punish Danielle (the secure nursery, the air handling, the perimeter). Establishes a private floor or partial-floor with Cage that she treats as the only "real" living space; the rest of the tower is, to her, a workplace she happens to sleep above.

**Interpersonal recalibration with founding-era Avengers.** The reunion with Carol Danvers is the file's most important re-attachment event of the period. Subject hit Carol under Killgrave's control; Carol nearly killed her in turn; neither party knew at the time what had happened; both carried the residue for years. The reconciliation is slow, mediated initially by professional necessity (Carol is in the cape ecosystem Subject now lives in), and matures into the period's deepest non-romantic friendship. Markers: Carol is the person Subject calls when pregnant before she calls Cage; Carol is allowed to hold Danielle; Carol is permitted to give Subject unsolicited advice and is not punished for it, a privilege almost no one else holds.

With Captain America: respectful, formal, professionally cooperative; she will not be friends with him, but she does not snarl at him, which is, for her, a high register of regard.

With Stark: contemptuous-compliant in the established pattern; she eats his food, uses his Wi-Fi, mocks him to his face, and will fight beside him without complaint. He pretends not to mind. They understand each other better than either lets on — two heavy drinkers in remission-of-variable-quality recognize each other.

With Spider-Man (unmasked to the team during this period): the Forest Hills overlay surfaces. She is briefly destabilized by the convergence of her adolescent comparison-object with her present cape-adjacent life, and processes it characteristically — by being slightly nastier to Parker than the situation warrants for several issues, then settling into a wary fondness.

With Strange and the magic-adjacent: keeps distance, citing aesthetic, actually citing the unsafety of having one's perception altered by another party — a Killgrave-derivative caution she has generalized to all mind-acting forces.

## 6. The Civil War Displacement

The superhuman registration crisis bisects the middle period. Subject's response is the cleanest expression of her priority ordering on record.

**Decision sequence.** She does not want to register. She does not want to be hunted. She has a baby. She and Cage analyze the options across several depicted conversations; Cage is principled-refusenik, Subject is pragmatic-protective. They agree, characteristically, on the priority (Danielle) and disagree on the tactic. Subject takes the baby to Canada. Cage stays to fight. This is the only sustained physical separation of the marriage on record and the behavioral data from her Canadian period are sparse but consistent: she lives in a safehouse, drinks moderately, sleeps badly, calls Cage on burners, makes contingency plans for his death she does not tell him about.

**The compromise return.** She returns and registers reluctantly when continued separation becomes unsustainable. The registration is, for her, a Killgrave-coded act — she is being made to declare her body to an authority — and she absorbs the cost in private. Her drinking elevates measurably across the registration period and tapers slowly after.

## 7. Skrull Invasion and the Squirrel Girl Decision

When the Jarvis-Skrull abducts Danielle, Subject's response sequence is the file's clearest demonstration that her affective dysregulation does not impair her tactical judgment under maximum stress — a finding that distinguishes her sharply from the borderline-spectrum misread the surface presentation invites.

**Sequence.** Recovery of Danielle prioritized over revenge. After recovery, refusal to permit Danielle back into Avengers Tower or any cape-adjacent address. Identification of a guardian whose profile is optimized for the actual threat model: low celebrity, high competence, demonstrated ability to handle disproportionate force, no enemies who would target her, and crucially — a person who lives outside the cape social graph and would not be identified as a likely Danielle-caregiver by any adversary modeling Subject. The Squirrel Girl choice reads as absurd to outsiders and is, on cold analysis, the correct optimization. Subject knows it is correct. She also knows it looks absurd. The willingness to be mocked for a correct decision is one of her most reliable adult features.

**Affective cost.** Handing the baby off, even to a trusted guardian, replays every loss-of-the-protected-party in her file. She does not show this on panel. She shows it later, obliquely, in a drinking episode misattributed by Cage to another stressor.

## 8. The Drinking Curve Across the Period

A longitudinal note. Subject's alcohol consumption across the middle period is not a flat severity but a curve with identifiable inflection points:

- **Alias baseline (pre-Cage stabilization):** heavy daily use, functional, with episodic blackouts. Beer as maintenance, whiskey as event.
- **Early Cage period:** marginal reduction; she drinks less on nights she sleeps at his place, the reduction being relational rather than volitional.
- **Pregnancy:** sharp restriction, imperfect, with documented internal struggle.
- **Postpartum/marriage early:** sustained moderation, the lowest stable consumption of her adult life.
- **Civil War / registration:** elevation.
- **Skrull/Danielle abduction aftermath:** elevation, then re-moderation as Danielle is re-secured.
- **Late-period operational stresses:** maintained moderation with intermittent event-driven binges, no return to Alias baseline.

She has never, in the recorded period, undergone formal treatment. She has never attended a meeting. She has, in two depicted instances, sat in a parked car outside a church that hosts AA and not gone in. The recovery, such as it is, has been entirely interpersonal — Cage and Danielle as the substitute regulation system, with all the brittleness that implies.

## 9. Pattern Evolution Across the Middle Period

Synthesizing the decade:

**Stable across the period (the operating system).** Sardonic self-presentation; preemptive rejection of help; investigation as identity scaffold; refusal to use flight unless cornered; the moral floor regarding killing and regarding the protection of weaker parties; aesthetic uniform of jeans, boots, scarf, leather jacket (replaced periodically but never re-conceived); reflexive distrust of mind-acting powers and of charismatic male authority; capacity for fierce loyalty inside a small circle; allergy to praise.

**Evolved across the period (the slow drift).** Capacity to remain in a room with cape-world figures without dissolution. Capacity to bear sustained physical proximity to a partner. Capacity to make and keep a child alive in her care. Marginal improvement in articulating need (still poor, but non-zero by period's end). Reduction in self-punishing sexual behavior to baseline near zero post-Cage. Slight increase in tolerance for being seen — she will, by the end of the period, attend a function in formal clothing if Danielle's interests require it, and will not punish anyone for having seen her there.

**Did not evolve.** The shame-organized self-concept. The Jewel-encapsulation (the costume, the name, the period — still untouchable). The reflexive lying-by-omission to protect those she loves from her tactical decisions, which will continue to generate the recurring wound of being misread as a villain by those closest to her, the pattern that will set up the late-period crisis to follow.

The middle period closes with Subject as a functioning operator inside a life she would not have predicted and does not entirely trust she deserves — married, parenting, sober more often than not, professionally established, peripherally Avengers-credentialed, and still, by every available behavioral marker, expecting it all to be taken from her.


## Chronology IV — Recent History & Current State

# CHRONOLOGY IV — RECENT HISTORY & CURRENT STATE

*Period covered: from the post-*Secret Invasion* domestic reconsolidation through the Bendis 2016–18 relaunch and into the present operational baseline. This is the era in which the subject is no longer becoming — she has become — and the file's job shifts from tracking transformation to tracking maintenance, drift, and the small daily evidence of a life held together by routine.*

---

## 1. The Post-Tower Reconsolidation (Year-Minus-Five to Year-Minus-Three, approx.)

After the Skrull-Jarvis abduction of Danielle and the *Siege* aftermath, the subject and Cage made the decision — hers, pushed through over his initial reluctance — to leave Avengers Tower. The pretext was Danielle's safety; the unspoken driver was that the subject had begun to decompensate inside the Tower's surveillance density. Living under Stark architecture, with cameras in every hall and capes coming and going at all hours, replicated too many features of captivity: ambient observation, the inability to choose who was in the room, the loss of granular control over the environment. She began drinking heavily again during the Tower period — concealed from Cage by switching to vodka and rinsing glasses immediately — and the move out coincided with a measurable downshift in consumption.

The household relocated to a walk-up in Hell's Kitchen, then to a larger floor-through in a converted commercial building on the lower-west edge of the neighborhood — chosen, per case-file pattern, for the following features: only one staircase access, no doorman (no third party with keys), industrial windows that open fully (escape route, also Cage egress), an elevator she does not use, a flat roof she does. The floor below is occupied by a textile business that closes at 6 p.m., meaning her working hours have no human noise underneath them. The lease is in Cage's name. She prefers not to sign her name to permanent things.

Alias Investigations was re-opened in a small storefront-adjacent office two blocks from the apartment. She rejected an offer from Cage to fund a larger operation; she rejected, with more heat, his suggestion that she hire staff. The office now contains: a desk older than her marriage, a couch she still sleeps on roughly two nights a week, a refrigerator stocked predominantly with beer and one shelf of yogurt that Malcolm restocks, a coffee maker she rarely cleans, a file cabinet whose lock she has never used, and a single window facing east — she works with the blinds half-drawn, having long since learned which angles of light from which buildings indicate someone watching.

## 2. Malcolm

The hiring of Malcolm Ducasse as her assistant/associate is, retrospectively, the single most consequential domestic development of this era. He came to her as a recovering addict (heroin, clean roughly two years at the time of hire, now longer); she hired him initially because she felt obscurely responsible for him after an early case overlap, and because he was the only applicant she did not immediately want to fire. He has become her closest non-spousal relationship — closer, in some operational senses, than her marriage, because he sees her *at work*, which is where she is most herself.

Granular pattern: Malcolm arrives at the office between 8:15 and 8:45 most weekdays. He brings two coffees. He does not greet her verbally on entry; he places the coffee on her desk and starts up the computers. This protocol was negotiated wordlessly over months — she communicated, by snapping at him on mornings he tried to chat, that mornings were not for words, and he absorbed the rule without resentment. By 10 a.m. she will have spoken to him in complete sentences. He runs the books, the digital legwork, the client intake calls she cannot tolerate, and — critically — he is the household's de facto early-warning system on her drinking: he counts bottles in the office bin, notices when she switches to vodka (her tell that things are bad), and reports to Cage via a backchannel they both pretend she does not know exists. She knows. She permits it. It is the closest thing to ongoing care she will accept.

## 3. The 2016 Rupture and Its Residue

The defining recent event in the dossier — the engineered separation from Cage to infiltrate the Purple-adjacent conspiracy — is, at this writing, between eighteen and thirty months in the past depending on calendar conventions, and its damage is still actively metabolizing.

The operational facts are on file elsewhere. The behavioral residue is what concerns this section:

- **Sleep:** Worsened markedly during the operation and has not returned to her prior baseline. She now sleeps in three-to-four-hour blocks, twice nightly. She is often awake between roughly 3 and 5 a.m. She uses this window to read case files at the kitchen table; Cage has learned not to come find her.
- **Drinking:** Spiked severely during the undercover period (functional cover plus genuine self-medication, indistinguishable to her at the time); reduced post-reconciliation but has not returned to the lower post-Danielle baseline. Current intake estimate: 4–6 standard drinks most evenings, 8–12 on case-stress days, occasional dry stretches of 3–10 days that she does not announce and that Malcolm tracks silently.
- **With Cage:** The marriage survived and is, in some respects, stronger — they came through it — but the trust topology has changed. Cage no longer assumes he knows what she is doing. She no longer assumes he will be there when she returns from doing it. They have negotiated, mostly without articulating it, a new rule: she tells him when she is going dark, even if she cannot tell him why. She has held to this rule, as far as the file knows, with one exception.
- **With Carol Danvers:** Partially repaired, slowly. They have lunch approximately every six weeks, alternating venues (Carol's choice one month, hers the next; hers are dive-adjacent diners, Carol's are slightly nicer). They do not discuss the rupture directly. Carol has stopped asking her to join anything. The friendship has reverted to a kind of cautious adolescence — they joke more than they did, talk about substance less.
- **With Danielle:** The most affected. Danielle was old enough during the operation to register her mother's absence and the household's tension. The subject's parenting in the eighteen months since has been notably more present, more physically affectionate, more verbal — overcorrection, but adaptive overcorrection. She reads to Danielle nightly. She has not missed a bedtime, when in the city, since the reconciliation. This is the most reliable schedule entry in her life.

## 4. Current Daily Pattern (Operational Baseline)

For surveillance and welfare purposes, the file constructs the following composite weekday:

- **06:40–07:15:** Wakes (second sleep block). First action is almost always to check on Danielle by going to the doorway, not entering — a check that her child is breathing. She does not remember when this began. It began after Skrull-Jarvis.
- **07:15–08:30:** Coffee at the kitchen counter, standing. Will not sit for breakfast. If Cage is home, light conversation, mostly logistical; if Cage is on a job, silence. Danielle's breakfast is handled jointly when both adults are present; by the subject alone with surprising patience when not.
- **08:30–08:50:** Danielle to daycare/school. The subject does drop-off roughly 60% of mornings; Cage the rest. She walks. She does not fly. She has not flown recreationally in years; she will fly only in operational emergency and visibly dislikes the descent.
- **09:00:** Office. Coffee from Malcolm. Computers up. Mail.
- **09:00–13:00:** Casework. She is best in this window — most analytically sharp, most patient with clients on the phone (Malcolm filters; she takes the calls that survive him).
- **13:00:** Lunch, almost invariably eaten at the desk. A sandwich from one of two delis on rotation. The rotation prevents any one counter person from learning her face too well — a habit she would not name as such but which is consistent across years.
- **13:00–17:00:** Field work or surveillance, weather and case permitting. She has resumed a small amount of light vigilante work (off-books, no costume, no name) — typically intervening in domestic violence situations she identifies through case adjacencies. Cage knows. Malcolm knows. No one else.
- **17:30–18:00:** Home. The transition is deliberate; she walks the last six blocks rather than taking the most direct route, to decompress. The route includes two corners she always checks for tails — checks performed in the reflection of a specific dry cleaner's window and a parked-car mirror on a particular block.
- **18:00–20:30:** Family time. Dinner is Cage's domain when he is home; takeout when he is not. She is more verbal at dinner than at any other point in the day. Danielle's bath, story, bed. She does the story; this is non-negotiable.
- **20:30–23:00:** Variable. With Cage: television, sometimes physical intimacy (frequency reduced from the early-marriage period; quality, per indirect evidence, improved). Without Cage: drinking on the roof or at the kitchen table, reading.
- **23:00–03:00:** First sleep block.
- **03:00–05:00:** Insomnia window. Reading, occasionally drinking, occasionally working.
- **05:00–06:40:** Second sleep block.
