*End BEHAVIORAL ATLAS. The subject is, across every condition catalogued above, rec


## Daily Life, Routines & Rituals

# UATU DOSSIER — SECTION 6: DAILY LIFE, ROUTINES & RITUALS

*What a week of physical and electronic surveillance on the subject would actually record, mapped against two decades of behavioral telemetry from the canonical corpus.*

---

## 6.1 The Operating Premise

The subject is not a creature of schedule. She is a creature of **rut** — a distinction worth making early, because the file would otherwise mis-read her. A scheduled life is forward-organized: tasks, calendars, anticipated needs. A rut is backward-organized: the same physical paths, the same five bars, the same chair, the same coffee place, the same three coats, because variation costs energy she has allocated elsewhere. She conserves her decision-making for cases and for parenting. Everything else is on the cheapest possible autopilot.

This is consistent across all observed periods. *Alias*-era Jessica, pre-Cage, runs the rut in a one-bedroom-plus-office configuration. *Pulse*/early-marriage Jessica runs a modified rut across two locations (the office on West 46th-ish; whatever apartment she and Cage hold at the time). New Avengers-era Jessica runs a compressed, anxious version of the rut inside Avengers Tower / Stark facilities, visibly uncomfortable. 2016-relaunch Jessica reverts almost identically to the *Alias* rut, as though twelve in-universe years collapsed into a single bad week.

The rut is the regulation strategy. It is the dysthymic-PTSD body's solution to the daily problem of producing a working adult from a person who, on most mornings, would prefer not to be one.

---

## 6.2 The Morning Window (approx. 09:30–11:30, variable)

She does not have a wake time in the civilian sense. She has a **threshold** — the point at which lying on the couch becomes more aversive than standing up. Surveillance footage would show movement between 09:30 and 11:30 on most days, with outliers in both directions: a 06:00 wake on a case day where she fell asleep at 22:00 sober (rare, distinguishable by the absence of the prior night's bottle in the trash), and a 14:00 wake after a heavy bender or a Killgrave-coded stressor.

The first ten minutes are diagnostic and would be filmed without sound:

1. She sits up on the couch or on the bed — interchangeable, she sleeps wherever she fell — without stretching. No yoga, no mobility work; her enhanced physiology makes the cost-benefit on stretching subjectively zero, though a clinician would note the absence of any morning interoceptive ritual as part of the broader pattern of somatic neglect.
2. She locates her phone. This is the first object touched almost every day. She checks (a) missed calls, (b) the time, (c) — only since Danielle's birth — whether Cage has texted about the child.
3. She locates cigarettes, in the *Alias* period; coffee, in all periods. The smoking is heavy through *Alias* (~ 3/4 pack/day inferred from on-panel frequency), reduced sharply during pregnancy, never fully resumed at prior volume.
4. She walks to the bathroom and urinates with the door open if she lives alone, closed if Cage or a guest is in the apartment. This is not modesty; it is a learned accommodation. Her own native setting is open.
5. She drinks water from the tap, cupped in her hand or directly from the faucet, before any other liquid. This is the single piece of self-care she performs reliably and unconsciously — likely a body-level residue of waking from a months-long coma dehydrated.

She does not shower in the morning unless meeting a client or going somewhere with Cage. Shower frequency averages 2–3x/week in the *Alias* period, climbing to near-daily during cohabitation with Cage and during the infant phase with Danielle (when she is touched constantly and notices her own smell more). Hair is washed less often than the body — every 4–6 days — and air-dried. No blow dryer has ever been observed in her possession.

Breakfast is functionally non-existent. Coffee, black, two to four cups, brewed in a drip machine whose carafe she does not wash between uses; the carafe accumulates a tannic ring that she scrapes with her thumbnail when bored on a phone call. On case days where she eats before noon, the food is either: a bagel from the corner deli (always the same deli, always either plain with butter or everything with cream cheese — she does not vary), a slice of pizza re-heated in the oven (she does not own a functioning microwave for long stretches), or — post-Danielle — whatever toast or cereal Cage has put in front of her.

---

## 6.3 The Office (Alias Investigations)

The address has moved at least twice over the publication run; the **interior arrangement** is invariant.

- Desk facing the door. This is non-negotiable. She will not work with her back to an entrance. A surveillance team would note that on the one or two occasions she has been forced into a hotel desk facing a wall, she has moved the chair to sit on the desk's near edge instead.
- The frosted-glass door with the stenciled name. She has, on at least two recorded occasions, considered changing the name; she has not.
- A landline. She keeps a landline well past the period when a comparable PI in New York would have abandoned it. The landline is for clients who do not want a mobile trail and for the answering machine, which she uses as a screening device — letting callers talk while she decides whether to pick up. The outgoing message is curt: name of the business, instruction to leave a message. No "have a nice day."
- A filing cabinet she actually uses. Despite the surface chaos, her case files are organized — paper, alphabetical by client surname, with a separate locked drawer for cases involving powered subjects. The lock is for show; she could rip it open one-handed and she knows anyone determined could too. The point of the lock is the threshold it sets: opening it is a decision.
- A bottle in the bottom drawer (whiskey, mid-shelf; the brand drifts but is never top-shelf — she associates expensive liquor with the cape world she scorns). In sober stretches the bottle is still there. Removing it would be a statement; keeping it is a different statement; she prefers the ambiguous one.
- A couch. The couch is the most-used object in the office. She sleeps on it perhaps 40% of nights in the *Alias* period. It is upholstered in something dark enough to hide stains.
- No plants. No photographs on the desk until Danielle, after which one photograph appears, propped, not framed.

**Work pattern.** She does not keep office hours in the advertised sense. She arrives between 10:30 and noon, runs phones and paperwork until roughly 15:00, then either goes out on a case (surveillance, knock-and-talks, courthouse records) or — if no active case — drinks coffee and reads tabloids, which she purchases at the same newsstand and which serve as both passive intel-gathering on the cape world and a private joke against it.

She prefers cases that require **walking around the city and looking at things**. She dislikes purely computer-based work and will outsource it to someone (in the *Alias* period, by going to a guy; in later periods, to Malcolm) whenever possible. Surveillance ratio across cases would skew heavily to foot — she walks rather than cabs, and almost never takes the subway during work hours, citing crowds; the file should read this as a Killgrave-derivative crowd aversion, not a preference.

She is unusually good at the **stationary stakeout** because her trauma physiology has, perversely, calibrated her to extended hypervigilant stillness. She can sit in a parked car or a diner booth for six hours without fidgeting. She does not bring a book. She watches. She drinks coffee. She does not, contrary to genre cliché, eat sandwiches on stakeout — she finds the smell intrusive in a closed car.

She bills erratically and undercharges. A forensic accountant would find a pattern of waived fees for clients she has categorized internally as "deserves it" — single mothers, exploited workers, abuse survivors — and aggressive billing of clients she has categorized as "deserves worse" — cheating husbands hiring her to flip the script, corporate counsel, anyone who arrives in a suit she identifies as expensive. Her business is, by any rational metric, mismanaged. It nevertheless persists.

---

## 6.4 The Midday and the Diet

Lunch, when taken, is between 13:00 and 15:00 at one of perhaps four locations within a six-block radius of the office. She rotates among them not for variety but to space out her overuse of any single waitstaff's patience. She tips well — disproportionately so — in cash. This is a tell: a person who has been treated as worthless tipping service workers as though to retroactively correct the universe's ledger.

Her diet is, across the entire corpus, **functionally adolescent**. Pizza, Chinese takeout (usually lo mein, sometimes General Tso's), deli sandwiches, diner breakfasts taken at non-breakfast hours, the occasional burger. Vegetables enter primarily as garnish or as the lettuce on a sandwich. She does not cook. Cage cooks, and during cohabitation her vegetable intake measurably increases; in his absence it collapses. She drinks soda — Coke, not Pepsi, observable across multiple panels — when not drinking coffee or alcohol. Water consumption is low and irregular, the morning tap-handful aside.

Despite this, her physical conditioning is excellent, a function of the powers rather than the lifestyle. A medical file would note the dissonance: the body of an athlete inside the habits of a chain-smoking grad student.

---

## 6.5 The Afternoon → Evening Transition

This is the most variable period and the most diagnostic. Three observed modes:

**Mode A — On a case.** She works through, often skipping dinner, returning to the office after dark to write up notes by hand on yellow legal pads. The handwriting is small, slanted, fast; she crosses out rather than erases. She talks to herself in a low mutter while working through a problem; surveillance audio would catch fragments — *"no, but then he —"*, *"why would she — "* — half-sentences she finishes in her head. She is most regulated in this mode. Drinking is moderated, almost reverent: a single whiskey at the desk while writing, refilled twice across two hours, not more, because the case requires her.

**Mode B — Between cases.** This is the dangerous mode. She has no external structure and her own internal structure has been hollowed by years of suppression. She drifts to a bar — Josie's recurs in the wider Hell's Kitchen continuity and she has been seen there; in the *Alias* period she has a closer bar she does not name on panel but which the file should track by silhouette and bartender. She drinks at a pace that begins social and becomes solitary even when the seat next to her is occupied. She has a tell here: when she crosses from social to solitary drinking, she removes her jacket and drapes it over the back of the stool, claiming territory and signaling unavailability simultaneously. The bartender learns to stop checking in.

**Mode C — Domestic (Cage period, Danielle period).** She comes home. "Home" is whatever apartment they currently hold; she has been resistant to nesting, leaving boxes unpacked for months, but Cage has been an anchoring counter-force. Evening behavior: takes Danielle from Cage or the sitter, holds the child against her left shoulder (consistent across years — left-shoulder hold for soothing, right-shoulder for transport), walks the apartment with her. She talks to Danielle in full adult sentences, no baby-voice, profanity included until Danielle reaches an age at which she begins selectively (and incompletely) censoring. Dinner is whatever Cage has made or ordered. She eats at the table only if he insists; left to her own devices she eats standing at the counter.

---

## 6.6 The Night

Insomnia is the most reliable feature of the file.

Bedtime — meaning the time she lies down with intent to sleep — averages 01:30 to 03:00 across the *Alias* period, drifting earlier (23:30–01:00) during stable cohabitation with Cage and post-Danielle, and snapping back to *Alias* parameters during stress arcs. Time to actual sleep: variable, often 45–90 minutes after lying down. She does not read in bed. She does not look at her phone for long stretches in bed (this is a holdover from the pre-smartphone *Alias* era that has not fully updated; she remains a person who associates bed with either sleep or sex, not screens).

She self-medicates the insomnia in tiered order: (1) work harder during the day; (2) drink; (3) sex, if a partner is present and willing; (4) lie awake. Surveillance would record her getting up two or three times a night on bad nights — water, bathroom, sometimes a circuit of the apartment checking locks. The lock-checking is post-Killgrave and persistent. She knows intellectually that locks would not have stopped him and would not stop most of her current threats; she checks them anyway. On the worst nights she stands at a window for ten to twenty minutes without moving, looking down at the street.

Nightmare frequency cannot be directly observed but is implied by morning behavior: on certain mornings (perhaps 1–2 per week, more during stress arcs) she emerges from sleep with a sharp inhalation rather than the usual gradual surfacing, sits up immediately, and walks to the bathroom to splash water on her face before doing anything else. This is the only ritual that displaces the morning phone-check.

She does not, on panel, ever describe a nightmare to another person. Cage has likely been present for many; he has the courtesy not to ask.

---

## 6.7 Habits of Place — the Geographic Rut

A pin-map of a typical week would cluster densely within a narrow Manhattan corridor:

- **Office.** West side, midtown-ish, walkable from a number of subway lines she does not use.
- **Apartment(s).** Variable address, consistent type: walk-up, exposed brick or equivalent unrenovated period detail, working fire escape (she will not rent a unit without an external escape route — operational, not aesthetic).
- **The bar.** One primary, two secondary. She does not drink at hotel bars, will not enter a club, refuses any venue with a velvet rope on principle.
- **The deli.** Coffee and bagels. She nods at the counterman; he knows her order.
- **The newsstand.** Tabloids, cigarettes (in the smoking years), occasionally a pack of gum.
- **A diner.** For mid-afternoon eggs and the rare formal meeting with a client who needs to be met somewhere public.
- **Police precincts and the courthouse.** As needed for case work; she has functional, transactional relationships with low-level clerks she has tipped or favored over years, and adversarial ones with most uniformed officers.
- **Daily Bugle / Alias Investigations crossover (Pulse period).** A bullpen environment she physically hated; surveillance would record an unusually high frequency of stairwell and bathroom breaks during her Bugle stint, consistent with sensory-overload escape.
- **Avengers Tower (New Avengers period).** She rarely leaves the residential floors, avoids the training and command spaces, and is observable at odd hours in the kitchen — the most domestic, least heroic room.

She does not go to Central Park except with Danielle. She does not go to Brooklyn except for cases. She has not, in any observable instance, taken a walk for the sake of taking a walk.

---

## 6.8 Dress and Grooming

A standing wardrobe of perhaps a dozen functional items, cycled to exhaustion:

- Jeans, dark, two or three pairs in rotation, replaced individually when destroyed.
- T-shirts, mostly plain, dark colors, occasionally a band shirt of indeterminate provenance.
- A leather jacket — the *primary* jacket — worn nine months of the year. The same one for years; she repairs rather than replaces. A second, heavier coat appears in deep winter.
- Boots. Combat-style, broken in. She wears them inside the apartment until specifically asked not to.
- Underwear: practical, replaced reluctantly, never on display in a way that suggests it has been considered.
- No purse. Wallet in back pocket, phone in front, keys clipped to a belt loop. This is operationally significant: she does not want her hands occupied.
- One dress, owned reluctantly, for funerals and the rare formal occasion. She does not wear it well and knows it.

Makeup is functionally absent. She has worn it for undercover work and for one or two social events under duress; her face is otherwise bare. Nails are short, often bitten in the *Alias* period (a tell that subsides during cohabitation with Cage). Hair is cut at long intervals at a cheap salon; she has cut it herself with kitchen scissors at least once.

**Color avoidance.** The file should flag this directly: she does not wear purple. Not ever. Not lavender, not violet, not aubergine. A surveillance team should treat any deviation from this as a profound signal — either deliberate confrontation of the trauma (rare, deliberate, observable in posture) or evidence she is not herself (impersonation, mind-control, dissociative episode).

---

## 6.9 Communication Patterns

- **Phone calls:** answered selectively. She lets the machine pick up first if she does not recognize the number. With recognized numbers, she answers fast — by the second ring — for Cage, Malcolm, Danielle's caregivers, and emergency clients; slowly or not at all for Carol (variable by arc), Jameson, and authority figures.
- **Voicemails she leaves:** short, no greeting, no signoff. *"It's me. Call me."* The lack of self-identification assumes the recipient already knows her voice — which is both efficient and a small assertion of intimacy.
- **Texting (post-adoption):** terse. Lowercase. No punctuation if she can avoid it. Emojis: never.
- **Email:** treated as a filing system, not a conversation. Replies in hours-to-days.
- **In-person conversation:** more eye contact than people expect from someone of her demeanor — she watches faces, partly investigative habit, partly Killgrave-residual scanning for compulsion. She lets silences sit. She interrupts rarely, but cleanly, when she does.

---

## 6.10 Substance Pattern by Hour and Day

A composite week in the *Alias* baseline:

- **Coffee:** 4–6 cups between wake and 16:00.
- **Cigarettes (smoking years):** distributed across the day with concentration at three points — first one with morning coffee, mid-afternoon cluster during phone work, late-evening cluster while drinking.
- **Alcohol:** typically begins between 17:00 and 19:00 on a non-case day; on bad days as early as 14:00; on case days deferred to post-work, sometimes skipped entirely. Volume: 4–8 standard drinks on an average drinking day, 10+ on a bad one, with periodic blackouts. She does not drink wine. She does not drink cocktails with more than two ingredients. Beer or whiskey, occasionally vodka, never gin.
- **Water:** under-consumed.
- **Food:** under-consumed and badly distributed; calories cluster late.

Post-Danielle this profile compresses substantially but does not vanish. The structural change is that she now has a witness she will not perform her worst self in front of, which has done more for her sobriety than any clinical intervention could.

---

## 6.11 Ritual Behaviors (Non-Obvious)

Habits a long surveillance file would catch that a short one would not:

- **The doorway pause.** Entering her own apartment or office, she pauses for half a beat in the doorway and scans the room before fully entering. Sub-second, easily missed. Post-Killgrave checking for occupants.
- **The phone placement.** She sets her phone face-down on flat surfaces. Always. She does not want to see the screen light up.
- **The bottle inventory.** Whether sober or drinking, she knows precisely what is in her liquor cabinet at any moment. She has, on multiple occasions, identified a missing bottle within minutes of a guest leaving.
- **The Danielle check.** Post-parenthood, regardless of who is on childcare duty, she physically goes to where Danielle is at least once an hour while in the same building. She does not pick her up; she looks at her. This persists even when Danielle is plainly safe and asleep.
- **The Cage anchor touch.** When in the same room as Cage and feeling overwhelmed, she will, without comment, touch his arm or back briefly and move on. Frequency increases with stress. He has learned not to remark on it.
- **The mirror non-engagement.** She uses mirrors functionally — brushing teeth, checking for visible injuries before a meeting — and does not linger. She does not study her own face. This is a long-standing avoidance of the woman the mirror returns to her, who is older and harder than the Jewel-era one and who she has not fully reconciled with.
- **The case-file closing ritual.** When a case ends, she writes "CLOSED" on the file in block capitals with a black marker, replaces the file in the cabinet, and pours a drink. The drink is the ritual; she will do this even on sober stretches with water or coffee. The closure must be marked.
- **The lock-check sequence.** Same order every time: apartment door deadbolt, chain, window in the main room, fire escape window. She has been observed performing the sequence twice on bad nights.
- **The dial-and-hang-up.** On certain anniversaries — the accident, the day she escaped Killgrave (date never named on panel but inferable from sequencing) — she has been observed dialing numbers and disconnecting before they ring. The Joneses' number is one of them. There are others she has never identified to anyone.

---

## 6.12 What a Composite Surveillance Week Would Conclude

A team filing on the subject after seven days of observation, without context, would describe her as:

A woman in her mid-thirties to forties, operating a small business out of a midtown office, living in a rent-stabilized apartment within walking distance, partnered with a man and co-parenting a young child. Habits: heavy coffee, heavy alcohol (variably), historical heavy tobacco, poor diet, severe insomnia, no exercise routine but excellent baseline physical condition (anomalous). No church, no gym, no clubs, no hobbies, no vacations. Geographic footprint extremely narrow. Social network small and intensely loyal. Professional conduct competent but financially undisciplined. Personal conduct withdrawn, profane, hypervigilant in observable micro-behaviors (doorway pauses, lock-checks, phone-placement), with strong avoidance of one specific color (purple) and one specific category of public space (crowds). Affective range narrow in public, with rare and brief affectionate display reserved for the child and the partner.

A team with the full file would add: this is what a survivor of prolonged interpersonal captivity, with intact baseline cognition and an unmedicated chronic PTSD presentation, looks like when she has built a functioning life around the wound rather than through it. The rut is the prosthesis. It holds her up. Do not, under any operational pretext, disturb it without cause.

*— end Section 6 —*


## Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

# RELATIONSHIPS I — FAMILY & ORIGIN BONDS

*Section scope: every bond formed by blood, by adoption, by gestation, or by the irrevocable fact of the dead. Friends, lovers, professional confidants are filed in subsequent sections. This section covers the people who made her, the people who replaced them, the people she could not save, and the person she made.*

The organizing analytic claim of this section: **Jessica's family relationships are organized around a single ungrievable event (the Forest Hills accident) and a single ungrievable failure (her inability to be present at any of the four deaths that defined her childhood — father, mother, brother, and, in a different register, the self she had been the morning the car left the driveway).** Every subsequent family bond — adoptive, marital, maternal — is metabolized through that triple-and-a-half loss. She is not a person without a family; she is a person whose family has the specific gravity of erasure, and every living relative she now has, she has had to choose to be related to.

---

## 1. BRIAN CAMPBELL — Father (deceased)

**Biographical baseline.** Engineer by training; middle-class Forest Hills householder; per *Alias* flashback panels, depicted as a quietly absorbed man, the kind of father who drives the family vacation with both hands on the wheel and a paper map folded on the dashboard. Killed in the convoy collision when Jessica was approximately fifteen. He is the parent she has the fewest direct flashbacks about — which is itself the datum.

**Dynamic in life.** What canon supplies, and what surveillance-grade inference can extract from it, is a relationship that was loving in the unremarked, undemonstrative idiom of a 1990s suburban father: provider, fixer of household items, presence at dinner, weekend pancake-maker, prone to lectures about gas mileage and tire pressure. Jessica in the surviving flashback fragments is mid-adolescent eye-roll mode with him; the affection is mutual but routed through bickering about the radio station, the route, the length of the trip. This is the ordinariness she lost — and the ordinariness she has not allowed herself to romanticize, because to romanticize it would require her to feel it.

**Evolution post-mortem.** He has aged inside her, the way the dead age inside the bereaved — frozen at the dashboard but accruing the projected wisdom of every year she has had without him. She does not visit the grave on panel. She does not, in the corpus, say his name aloud often. When she does — usually in third-person construction ("my dad was an engineer," delivered flatly to establish background to a stranger) — the affect is sealed. The seal is the relationship now.

**Trust/safety register.** He is the template for one specific category of male: the competent, undemonstrative, non-coercive man whose attention is reliable. Luke Cage occupies this slot. So, in different proportions, do Malcolm (the loyalty), Matt Murdock at his best (the moral seriousness), and Scott Lang (the wanting-to-be-this for her, which she rejects, because Lang isn't actually that and she can tell). Brian is the unconscious sorting algorithm for which men she will let stand next to her.

**Failure mode.** She was the surviving passenger. The convoy hit the family car; she lived; he did not. The specific shape of the survivor-guilt is that she got *more* from the accident than she lost — powers, life, a future — while he got nothing, and the asymmetry is intolerable. She cannot make the math come out, so she does not run the calculation. The drinking, in part, is the not-running of the calculation.

**Leverage.** Operationally minimal in the present (he is dead and no enemy has weaponized his memory on panel). Psychologically immense: any narrative that frames her as a daughter who failed her father, or as a person whose life is undeserved relative to a dead man's, would land where she is undefended. The *Jessica Jones* 2016 arc's undercover-isolation logic — *I will burn every bond to do this alone* — has an unspoken substratum here: she has already survived the loss of everyone, once; she carries the muscle memory for it.

---

## 2. ALISA CAMPBELL — Mother (deceased)

**Biographical baseline.** Homemaker; the parent more frequently present in flashback dialogue, often in the kitchen, often in the car arguing about navigation. In the surviving fragments she reads as warm, slightly anxious, the sort of mother who packed snacks for a four-hour drive and worried aloud about Phillip's seatbelt.

*(Authorial note for the file: in later non-Bendis material a version of Alisa survives the accident in altered form; this dossier brackets that continuity as non-canonical to the Bendis-spine corpus on which our subject's psychology is built. Jessica, as she presents in the source material being mapped, believes and has always believed her mother died in the crash. The psychological reality is what we are tracking.)*

**Dynamic in life.** Adolescent friction of the standard kind, intensified by Jessica's already-irritable baseline. Flashback panels suggest the mother-daughter tension was the loudest thing in the family — Brian quiet, Phillip a child, Jessica and Alisa generating the household weather. Jessica was, at fifteen, rude to her mother in the small ways teenagers are rude; she has not forgiven herself for any of them.

**Evolution post-mortem.** Alisa is the parent Jessica re-encounters every time she looks at Danielle. The reparative drive structuring her motherhood — *be present in the way no one was present for me* — is also and more precisely *be present in the way I refused to let my mother be present for me in the last year of her life*. There is no panel where Jessica articulates this. The behavior articulates it: the way she holds Danielle, the way she has organized her late-period life around the child's physical safety, the way she has accepted Luke Cage's domestic competence rather than competing with it.

**Trust/safety register.** Alisa is the template for the woman-as-anchor slot — and that slot is largely vacant in Jessica's adult life, which is itself diagnostic. Carol Danvers occupies it in fits and starts; the friendship's repeated rupture-and-repair cycle reads, in part, as Jessica's inability to let any woman occupy her mother's empty chair without testing the chair until it breaks. The woman she most loved was taken in a single instant; she has not, since, allowed a woman to fully matter to her without periodically forcing the relationship to almost-end, as if rehearsing the loss to prove she can survive it again.

**Failure mode.** The last conversation. Canon does not show it explicitly, but the corpus implies it — a teenager's irritable nothing-words, a mother absorbing them, then the windshield. Jessica has been writing the apology for thirty years in the form of how she treats Malcolm when he is being kind to her: *I do not deserve this, please do not stop offering it, I will not say thank you.*

**Leverage.** Same structural shape as her father — operationally low, psychologically catastrophic if invoked precisely. Any antagonist who weaponized the maternal frame (a mother-figure mind-controlled, a maternal voice impersonating Alisa's) would route through her defenses fast. Killgrave, notably, never tried this — he was too narcissistic to imagine she had a mother who mattered more than him.

---

## 3. PHILLIP CAMPBELL — Younger Brother (deceased)

**Biographical baseline.** The youngest member of the original family; a child at time of death (younger than Jessica by enough years that the relationship was sibling-asymmetric, with her in the bossy-older-sister role). Killed in the same collision.

**Dynamic in life.** Sparse direct depiction; what is canonically established is the existence and the death. From the inference engine: the older-sister-to-younger-brother bond at the ages depicted is the bond in which Jessica was most uncomplicatedly the protector. Phillip is the only person in her original family she did not have adolescent friction with — he was too young to be a peer, too dependent to be an irritant, too admiring to be resented. He looked up to her. This was the only relationship in her teenage life in which she was, without ambivalence, the strong one.

**Evolution post-mortem.** Phillip is the ghost in every case involving a child, every protective rage triggered by a kid in danger, every moment in the corpus where Jessica's affect-flatness breaks. The Mattie Franklin case in *Alias* — a young woman, MGH-addicted, exploited — sits in the Phillip-shaped wound: someone younger, someone she was supposed to protect, someone consumed by forces too large for them. She works that case past the point of professional obligation because Phillip is in the room with her.

**Trust/safety register.** Phillip is the template for the I-am-the-shield slot. Danielle occupies it now, totally. Malcolm partially occupies it (younger man, recovering addict, in some sense in her care), which is part of why she lets him in: he triggers the protective drive that bypasses her defensive ones. Children in cases activate it instantly and unconditionally; this is documented across the *Alias* run.

**Failure mode.** She was the older sister and she did not protect him. The fact that no fifteen-year-old could have protected anyone from a head-on collision with a military convoy does not enter the calculation. The calculation is: *he was small and he trusted me and he died.* This is the deepest layer of the contamination-shame: she failed at the one role she had been uncomplicatedly good at.

**Leverage.** A child in danger collapses her tactical judgment. This is the predictability noted in the personality section — *threats to Danielle override every other tactical consideration; she becomes predictable here.* The structural source is Phillip. Any opponent who threatens a child she has taken responsibility for will get her to come, alone, fast, and stupid.

---

## 4. THE JONESES — Adoptive Parents

**Biographical baseline.** A family that took in the orphaned, hospitalized, recently-superpowered teenager after the accident. Canon is deliberately spare on them — first names rarely used, no extended scenes of family life, no flashback to a meaningful conversation. This sparseness is the data: Jessica has filed them, in her own narrative of her life, as a footnote.

**Dynamic in adolescence.** They were, by every available indicator, decent people who did a hard thing. They took a traumatized, mute, possibly dangerous teenager into their home and did not return her. Jessica has rewarded this by treating them, across the corpus, as not-her-family in a way that ranges from polite distance to active resentment. She kept her birth surname for years (filed initially as Jessica Campbell, later as Jones, the name-shift itself a slow concession). She did not tell them about the powers. She did not, per canon, tell them about Killgrave. She moved out as soon as she could and did not move back.

**Evolution.** Adult-era contact is depicted as minimal-to-nonexistent. They are not at her wedding to Cage on panel in a way the file would register; they are not at the hospital for Danielle's birth in any depicted moment; they are not the grandparents Danielle visits. Whether this is canonical absence or canonical estrangement, the functional reality is the same: the Joneses are not in her operational life.

**Trust/safety register.** Low — not because they did anything wrong but because they exist in the slot where her real parents should be, and the slot is radioactive. Every time they were kind to her at fifteen, the kindness was an indictment of the universe for not letting her real parents be the ones being kind. She could not metabolize their generosity; she could only resent its necessity.

**Failure mode (hers, not theirs).** She has spent her adult life with a surname she did not choose, given by people whose love she did not let in, because to let it in would have required her to admit that her real parents were never coming back. The Jones name is, in this sense, a daily small monument to a refusal to grieve. She walks under it; she does not look at it.

**Leverage.** Operationally near-zero (no apparent enemy has identified them as targets). Psychologically: an antagonist who confronted her with the moral fact of how she has treated them would land a clean strike. She has not paid the debt and she knows it.

---

## 5. ZEBEDIAH KILLGRAVE — The Purple Man (filed under family by functional logic)

**Rationale for inclusion in Family & Origin Bonds.** He is not blood. He is filed here because in the eight months of captivity he occupied, by force, the structural position of the only intimate authority figure in her adult life; because the relationship was the inverse-template against which every subsequent intimacy has been measured; and because in the file's longitudinal logic, he is the second father — the one who taught her what proximity to a powerful other can do.

**Dynamic during captivity.** Total. He selected her clothes, her food, her speech, her postures, her targets. He compelled her to want him, then to despise wanting him, then to want him again — the loop is the wound. He made her stand for hours. He made her watch. He made her beg. He did not touch her sexually in the act-completion sense; he did the worse thing, which was to make her own will the instrument of her violation. This is the canonical clarification across *Alias* #24–28 and it is the precise reason her PTSD takes the shape it takes.

**Evolution post-captivity.** He is in the room with her at all times. The psychic defense the X-Men installed prevents him from controlling her again; it does not prevent him from existing in her head. Purple-coded stimuli — the color, certain cadences of male confidence, anyone who tells her what she really wants — produce the documented freeze-rage-shame cycle. The 2016 arc returns him in displaced form (the Purple Children) and the displacement does not soften the effect; she still goes undercover alone, still cannot speak, still alienates everyone, still wins by mastering her own affect. The shape of her victory is always the same: she refuses to be baited, and the refusal costs her every relationship in the room.

**Trust/safety register.** Negative-infinite. He is the calibration point against which all male attention is measured, which is why Cage's refusal to direct her, advise her against her will, or "handle" her is not merely pleasant but structurally load-bearing for the marriage.

**Failure mode of the bond.** That it exists at all. She would, if she could, excise him from the architecture of her psychology; she cannot; she has accreted enough function around the wound to live, but the wound does not close.

**Leverage.** This is the file's single most important entry under leverage, for any actor. Purple-coded stimuli. Voice-based compulsion. Any orchestrated situation in which her consent is engineered rather than asked. Any framing in which she is mistaken for a villain and cannot explain herself. These are the four switches; any one of them, pulled cleanly, decompensates her. All four pulled at once is the *Alias* finale, and she survived it by a margin so narrow that the survival itself is part of the trauma.

---

## 6. LUKE CAGE — Husband (filed in Relationships II; cross-referenced here as co-parent)

**Cross-reference note.** Cage's full mapping is in the next section. He appears here only in his capacity as the co-constituent of the family unit she has built — the second author of Danielle, the man whose surname she has not taken (she remains Jones; the refusal to take a third surname is its own datum), the domestic counterweight whose competence allows her to be the parent she is.

**Family-systems function.** Cage is the regulator. Jessica is the vigilance; Cage is the ground. Danielle has, by structural luck and by both parents' deliberate work, the rarest configuration available to the child of two traumatized superhumans: one parent who scans the horizon and one parent who keeps the house warm. They have not always done this well — the *Civil War* flight to Canada, the Skrull-Jarvis abduction, the 2016 fake separation — but the structural assignment is stable.

**Leverage via Cage.** He is the second-most-predictable lever after Danielle. Threats to him route through her in the same shape, slightly attenuated by the knowledge that he can take more punishment than almost anyone alive. She is less afraid for him than for the child; she is more afraid of losing him than of losing almost anyone else.

---

## 7. DANIELLE CAGE — Daughter

**Biographical baseline.** Conceived in the *Pulse* era; born late *Pulse* / early *New Avengers*; named for Daniel Rand (Iron Fist), Cage's closest friend, a naming choice Jessica accepted without contest — a small but real index of her trust in Cage's chosen family. Infant through the bulk of the corpus; the child of two enhanced parents in a world that has repeatedly demonstrated its willingness to harm children of enhanced parents.

**Dynamic from gestation.** The pregnancy was psychologically destabilizing in a textbook way for a trauma survivor with hyperarousal: a body she did not fully trust hosting a body she could not protect, in an environment populated by enemies who had previously demonstrated the ability to take her body from her. The recurring fear was not abstract; it was Killgrave-shaped (*what if something gets inside me and uses me against this child*) and accident-shaped (*what if I am driving and the convoy comes*). She carried to term. The carrying was an act of will against the entire architecture of her psychology, and it deserves to be filed as such.

**Dynamic in infancy.** Hypervigilant attachment, but — and this is the critical clinical point — **not anxious-disorganized in transmission to the child**. Jessica has done the thing trauma-aware parents try to do and frequently fail at: she has metabolized her hypervigilance into environmental management (who holds the baby, where the baby sleeps, who knows where the baby is) rather than into the baby's nervous system. The Squirrel Girl nanny decision is the cardinal evidence: under maximum threat conditions, she made a choice that looked absurd and was in fact tactically optimal, because the optimality required her to think past her own panic. A worse mother would have clutched the child closer; Jessica handed her to the safest available person and walked toward the threat.

**Evolution.** Danielle is the slow reorganization of every drive in the personality section. The protective drive that re-built Jessica after Killgrave has, in Danielle, found its definitive object. The maternal presence-drive that is the unspoken apology to Alisa is here being paid. The shield-drive that is the unspoken apology to Phillip is here being exercised in real time, with a living child who is not going to die in a car if Jessica can help it.

**Trust/safety register.** Total in the direction Jessica → Danielle. The child is the one person Jessica will, without hesitation, accept help in protecting — she will call Cage, call Carol, call Squirrel Girl, call anyone, because the protection of the child overrides the pride that prevents her from accepting help in any other domain. This is the single largest behavioral exception to her isolation pattern in the entire corpus.

**Failure mode.** Predictability. As noted in the personality section, she becomes tactically transparent when Danielle is the stake. A sophisticated antagonist can route her into any trap by establishing credible threat to the child. She knows this; she has not been able to neutralize it; she has chosen, instead, to live with it as the price of being a present mother rather than a protected one.

**Leverage.** Maximal. There is no operator in this file's purview for whom a single lever is more total. The file recommends, under benevolent surveillance logic, that any actor with access to Danielle's location data treat that data as the most sensitive category of information about Jessica Jones — more sensitive than her home address, her case files, her psychiatric history, or the Killgrave-coded triggers. The child is the load-bearing wall.

---

## 8. THE ABSENT GRANDPARENTS / EXTENDED CAMPBELL FAMILY

**Canonical sparseness.** No aunts, uncles, grandparents, or cousins are meaningfully depicted in the corpus as having taken Jessica in, contested her custody, or remained in her life. This absence is itself the data: the orphaning was complete enough that no Campbell relative materialized to claim her, which is part of why the Joneses became the option they became, and part of why she did not have a soft place to land into in the year after the accident. She landed on the Joneses' floor, and she has been getting up from that floor ever since.

**Operational implication.** No extended-family lever exists for an antagonist to pull. The Campbell line, as a network, terminates at her. She is, in the genealogical sense, the surviving branch — and Danielle is the only continuation. This is part of the weight Danielle carries that Danielle does not yet know she carries: she is not only Jessica's daughter; she is, in the unspoken arithmetic, the entire Campbell future, restored.

---

## SYNTHETIC NOTE ON FAMILY ARCHITECTURE

The family file resolves to a single shape: **one catastrophic deletion (the Campbells), one refused replacement (the Joneses), one weaponized intrusion (Killgrave in the structural-father slot he had no right to), one chosen partner whose entire function is to be the inverse of the intruder (Cage), and one child who is simultaneously the reparation owed to the mother, the shield owed to the brother, the future owed to the father, and the only relationship Jessica has ever been in where she did not have to earn her own admittance.**

She is, in family terms, a person rebuilding a four-person household from one survivor, across thirty years, against her own defenses, with a husband who knows what she is doing and helps her do it. The rebuilding is incomplete. It will remain incomplete. The fact that it is happening at all, against the pressure of the Killgrave wound and the accident wound and the Joneses-shaped guilt, is the most important behavioral datum in the entire dossier.

*End Section. Continues: Relationships II — Chosen Family & Intimate Partners.*


## Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

# RELATIONSHIPS II — ALLIES, CHOSEN FAMILY & MENTORS

*Compiled cross-reference: Alias #1–28; Pulse #1–14; New Avengers vols. 1–2; Daughters of the Dragon backups; Bendis-run Jessica Jones #1–18; Defenders (2017); incidental appearances in Daredevil, Cage, Power Man and Iron Fist. UATU note: Subject's chosen-family circle is unusually small, dense, and durable; she has roughly six load-bearing relationships and treats almost everyone else as either case-work, professional acquaintance, or noise. The smallness is itself diagnostic — high vetting threshold, low maintenance bandwidth, and a deliberate firewalling strategy designed so that no compromise of one person cascades into compromise of the others.*

---

## 1. LUKE CAGE — *Husband; co-parent; only person granted unconditional standing.*

**Provenance.** First encounter occurs in her Knightress period — a brief rooftop team-up against bail-jumper Nick Pollard, followed by a one-night stand at a bar in Hell's Kitchen. She does not give him her real name. Years later, in *Alias* #1, she walks into his apartment above the gym, drinks until she can stop thinking, and has sex with him a second time; the morning-after sequence (Gaydos pages — three silent panels of her dressing in the dark, the cracked mirror, the un-flushed toilet) is the foundational somatic data on her sexual avoidance. She leaves without speaking. He does not pursue. This non-pursuit is the precise behavior that, six months later, makes her trust him.

**Architecture of the bond.** Cage is the only adult intimate she has ever permitted continuous physical proximity. The mechanism is specific: he is, by power-set, the one body in Manhattan she cannot accidentally hurt and that cannot be coerced by Killgrave (unbreakable skin precludes pheromonal pinprick delivery in some readings; more importantly, his calm is unbreakable). He functions as a somatic buffer — being near him lowers her resting hyperarousal in a way that nothing else, including alcohol, achieves. Note the recurring physical motif across *New Avengers*: she sleeps against his chest, face toward the door; he sleeps on his back. She is the one who must see the entrance. He accepts this without comment for years.

**Evolution.** Phases:
- *Sexual transactional (Alias #1).* She uses him; he lets himself be used while reading her clearly.
- *Reluctant dating (Alias #26 onward).* He insists, gently, on being told what happened with Killgrave. She tells him. He does not flinch. This is the moment she begins to attach.
- *Pregnancy and the proposal sequence (Pulse).* He proposes; she initially refuses, marries him later on her own terms (City Hall, no ceremony, witnesses Carol and Matt). She wears jeans.
- *Avengers Tower cohabitation.* Domesticity is destabilizing to her — she chafes at the security, the staff, the openness — but she stays because Cage's reading is correct: this is the safest envelope for Danielle.
- *Civil War split-then-reunion.* They flee to Canada together. The shared decision to defy registration is the first time she experiences ideological alignment with a partner as relief rather than threat.
- *Bendis 2016 fake separation.* She enacts a months-long deception, allowing him to believe she has left him and taken Danielle. The arc is the closest she has come to repeating the precise structure of the Avenger-hospital wound *to him* — and he absorbs the truth, when revealed, with a controlled fury that includes forgiveness. They reconcile, but the file should note: the relationship now contains a known fracture line he has not forgotten.

**Trust / safety rating.** Maximum within subject's tolerance ceiling. She tells him roughly 80 percent of operationally significant things, in delay (24–72 hours typical), and almost never in real time. He has learned to receive late disclosure without punishing it, which is a learned skill not a temperament.

**Failure mode.** When overwhelmed, she does not lean toward him — she lies to protect him. The lie is always tactical, always altruistic in her construction, and always partially a defense against being seen mid-decompensation. He has, twice now, had to choose to forgive a unilateral protective deception. A third would test the structure.

**Leverage (analytical, not exploitative).** Cage is the single largest behavioral lever in subject's environment. Threats to him are processed as threats to self; threats from him (disapproval, withdrawal) bypass her defenses entirely. Any actor seeking to predict her conduct should model Cage's state first and subject's second.

---

## 2. DANIELLE CAGE — *Daughter; reparative object; the relationship that re-organized her psyche.*

**Provenance.** Conceived during the *Pulse* run; carried to term despite obvious risk discourse around superhuman fetal viability and her own ambivalence. The pregnancy reveal scene (Pulse #1, the bathroom, three positive sticks lined on the sink, her sitting on the floor for a wordless full page) is the visual canon for her affective stuckness at the threshold of motherhood.

**Architecture.** Danielle is the only person in the file with whom subject is unguarded by default. The behavioral evidence is unambiguous: subject's voice register drops, her face muscles soften (Gaydos consistently draws her eyes wider and her mouth unfixed when holding the child), her sentence length shortens, profanity drops to near zero, and she touches the child constantly — hair, foot, cheek — small contact loops that appear to function as co-regulation for *her* as much as for the infant.

**Evolution.** Infant phase: hypervigilant maternal anxiety, sleep checks every 40–90 minutes per *New Avengers* incidentals, refusal to leave Danielle with non-powered minders. The Squirrel Girl childcare decision (*New Avengers* #34 vintage) reads as comedic but is, on file, a rigorously optimized choice — a powered caretaker with no enemies, no glamour, and a documented lack of corruptibility. Subject's tactical reasoning about her child is the cleanest reasoning she does. Toddler phase (*Bendis 2016*): increased separation tolerance, increased terror about the world Danielle is growing into; the Purple Children arc weaponizes precisely this anxiety.

**Trust / safety.** Not a peer relationship; the rating frame doesn't apply. What is relevant: the existence of Danielle has installed a new top-of-stack value that overrides subject's older self-destructive drives. She drinks less, sleeps more, and takes fewer suicidal-grade tactical risks since the birth, with measurable exceptions when she believes risk to self is the only path to safety-for-Danielle.

**Failure mode.** Subject cannot tolerate uncertainty about Danielle's location. Loss-of-contact events produce immediate decompensation — freeze, then phone-and-fly behavior, then disproportionate force. She is *predictable* on this axis, which is otherwise rare for her.

**Leverage.** Catastrophic. Anyone with credible reach to Danielle owns her decision tree. She knows this and has, post-Skrull-Jarvis, built an unusually thick perimeter of compartmentalized safekeepers (Cage, Squirrel Girl, Misty Knight as named backstop, with a documented dead-drop protocol for emergency relocation).

---

## 3. CAROL DANVERS — *Oldest friend; structural opposite; the relationship that documents her capacity to love women.*

**Provenance.** Friendship predates *Alias*, predates Killgrave, predates Jewel — the canon implies they met in the cape-circuit early, became close, and that Carol was the friend Jessica was attempting to reach (via the wrong target, Scarlet Witch) when mind-controlled into the Avengers attack. The original wound therefore implicates Carol twice: as the body Jessica's hands nearly destroyed under coercion, and as the friend who (with the rest of the Avengers) did not realize what had happened.

**Architecture.** Carol is the only person in the file with whom subject sustains a non-romantic peer intimacy. Their interactions across *Alias* and *Pulse* exhibit a distinctive register: rapid-fire sardonic exchange, mutual ribbing on drinking habits (Carol's own AUD history is the unspoken connective tissue — they are two women in alcohol-soaked recovery-adjacent orbits, neither lecturing the other), and a comfort with silence that subject otherwise displays only with Cage.

**Evolution.**
- *Pre-Killgrave:* sisterly, evidence sparse but warm in flashback.
- *Post-Killgrave gap:* years of estrangement, by subject's election. Carol does not understand why subject vanished; subject cannot bear to explain.
- *Alias re-engagement:* Carol reappears, finds subject drunk and pregnant, and assumes — correctly — the role of the friend who shows up uninvited. This is the only person in the file permitted to enter without being asked.
- *Pulse / New Avengers:* Carol is Danielle's godmother in everything but ceremony. She visits.
- *Civil War rupture:* Carol pro-registration; subject and Cage anti. The friendship survives but accrues a permanent scar tissue layer.
- *Bendis 2016 deepening rupture:* Subject's faked-separation gambit alienates Carol again; reconciliation is partial.

**Trust / safety.** High-but-volatile. Subject trusts Carol's loyalty absolutely and her judgment selectively. Carol is the only person to whom subject has, on panel, said "I love you" without flinching (small-bar scene, *Alias* #22-adjacent).

**Failure mode.** Ideological divergence — Carol's institutional loyalties (Avengers, later cosmic command, later Alpha Flight) periodically place her on the wrong side of subject's anti-authoritarian default. Each divergence is processed by subject as a partial Killgrave-coded threat (a powerful figure asking her to comply against her own read), even when Carol is doing nothing of the sort. Subject is somewhat aware of this distortion but cannot fully correct for it.

**Leverage.** Moderate. Carol's disapproval is one of the few social signals subject still feels. Carol cannot order her to do anything; Carol can, by withdrawing, induce a self-correction within roughly two weeks.

---

## 4. MALCOLM DUCASSE — *Assistant; protégé; surrogate younger brother; her quiet success at parenting forward.*

**Provenance.** Introduced in the Marvel Cinematic adjacency, but in print canon (later Bendis-run *Jessica Jones*) functions as her on-the-ground assistant at Alias Investigations: a younger man, formerly in subject's debt for a Killgrave-related rescue, who has installed himself in her office and refuses to leave.

**Architecture.** Malcolm occupies the structural slot her dead brother Phillip would have occupied. The evidence is in the irritation register: subject snaps at Malcolm in a tone she uses with no other adult — the specific irritation of an older sibling at a younger one who is correct but annoying. She lets him answer her phone. She lets him see her hungover. She lets him correct her caseload. None of these permissions is extended to anyone else outside Cage.

**Evolution.** Initial reluctant tolerance; gradual on-boarding into operational trust; eventual delegation of low-grade casework; by late Bendis run, he is the only person who reliably knows her actual location during a working day.

**Trust / safety.** High-operational, medium-emotional. He does not get the trauma material; he gets the schedule. The compartmentalization is itself a form of care — she has decided he should not have to carry her history.

**Failure mode.** She underpays him, overworks him, and routinely withholds praise that he has earned. The relationship runs on his patience, which is finite but renewable. If he ever quit, she would not know how to ask him back.

**Leverage.** Low coercive; high informational. He is the cleanest single-source for predicting subject's movements 6–48 hours out.

---

## 5. MATT MURDOCK — *Counsel; ethical mirror; the only cape she respects without conditions.*

**Provenance.** Repeated professional collaboration across *Alias*, *Pulse*, and *Daredevil* tie-ins. He has served as her attorney in at least two documented matters and as a tactical consultant in several more.

**Architecture.** Murdock is the cape whose self-loathing register matches her own and whose Catholic-shame structure she finds intelligible without sharing. Their exchanges are notably honest — neither performs for the other. He reads her physiologically (heartbeat, breath) and she knows this and does not mind; it is one of the few asymmetric-knowledge relationships she tolerates, because his disclosure of his own condition has been equally costly.

**Evolution.** Professional → grudgingly social → occasional confidant on legal-ethical edge cases. Never sexual, despite circumstantial pressure; the boundary is mutual and respected.

**Trust / safety.** High in scope; narrow in domain. She trusts him with legal jeopardy, tactical questions, and the kind of moral reasoning Cage is not built for. She does not trust him with logistics or with Danielle.

**Failure mode.** Both of them spiral; neither is a good crisis resource for the other when both are in crisis simultaneously. They have learned, mostly, to stagger.

**Leverage.** Low direct; high via reputational standing. His endorsement of a course of action will move her more than most.

---

## 6. JESSICA DREW — *Sister-coded peer; the most physically affectionate adult bond.*

**Provenance.** Connection deepens through *New Avengers* tenure; the name-mirroring is treated, both in and out of canon, as joke and as bond. Drew is one of the few women subject has ever drunk with in a register that is celebratory rather than anesthetic.

**Architecture.** They share, structurally, a history of bodily violation by a hostile external agent (Killgrave; HYDRA/Skrull replacement). Neither has to explain to the other what it means to have one's will or body conscripted. This shared substrate produces an unusual physical ease — subject permits Drew to touch her shoulder, lean against her on couches, share cabs at close range — proximity tolerances she extends nowhere else outside Cage and Danielle.

**Trust / safety.** High emotional, medium operational. The Skrull-replacement revelation (Drew was a Skrull for an extended period) retroactively poisoned a band of memories and installed a permanent verification reflex on subject's part. She still loves Drew; she also, on first contact each time, runs a check.

**Failure mode.** Drew's espionage-world entanglements periodically generate threat surfaces subject cannot vet, which throttles operational trust regardless of emotional trust.

**Leverage.** Moderate. Drew is one of perhaps three people who can prompt subject to attend a social event she would otherwise refuse.

---

## 7. THE ADOPTIVE JONESES — *Family of record; emotional creditors she has never repaid.*

**Provenance.** Foster-then-adoptive family who took her in post-coma. Their on-panel presence is minimal — Bendis deliberately keeps them faded — but they are the ledger entry against which much of subject's guilt is balanced.

**Architecture.** Subject took their surname, accepted their housing, and never accepted their family-hood. She visits rarely. Calls less. Sends Danielle photos at irregular intervals. They have, by all evidence, behaved decently and patiently for decades.

**Trust / safety.** Functionally untested; she has not let them close enough to test.

**Failure mode.** The relationship is the largest documented site of subject's avoidance behavior outside the Jewel-costume taboo. Approaching them activates the displacement-guilt of being-alive-when-her-real-family-is-not. She manages this by minimizing contact, which preserves her stability at the cost of their dignity.

**Leverage.** Low direct; high latent. A health crisis on their end would force a reckoning she has spent decades deferring.

---

## 8. SCOTT LANG — *Failed dating attempt; documented good-faith approach she could not metabolize.*

**Provenance.** *Alias* mid-run dating arc. Lang is decent, divorced, a fellow ex-cape, ambivalent about the life — structurally near-ideal for her.

**Architecture and failure.** She sabotages it. Specifically: she stands him up, she drinks before dates, she discloses nothing of substance, and in the climactic *Alias* sequence she breaks down rather than let the relationship deepen. The arc is diagnostic: presented with a non-threatening, non-coercive, age-appropriate suitor, she cannot proceed. Cage works precisely because Cage was *not* offered in this register — he arrived through transgression and silence, not courtship.

**Disposition.** Friendly residual; no recurrence attempted. Lang remains, in her internal catalogue, the evidence that the problem is hers and not the world's.

**Leverage.** Negligible.

---

## 9. PETER PARKER — *Childhood touchstone; symbolic figure more than relationship.*

**Provenance.** Forest Hills high school overlap. They were not friends. He did not know she existed in any meaningful way. She knew exactly who he was, in the particular envy-register of an adolescent girl who is unremarkable in proximity to someone visibly remarkable.

**Architecture.** The relationship is asymmetric to the point of being nearly one-sided. In adult interactions across *Pulse* (where she covers superhuman beat at the Bugle and overlaps with Parker's photo work), she is brusque-protective toward him in a way that surprises both of them. The childhood envy has resolved into something closer to fond exasperation; she sees, now, what the cost of his life is, and she is unwilling to add to it.

**Trust / safety.** Moderate-distant. She would not confide; she would protect.

**Failure mode.** She still cannot quite be neutral about him — there is residual prickliness in their exchanges that neither has bothered to name.

**Leverage.** Low.

---

## 10. MISTY KNIGHT — *Operational peer; backup childcare; the only other woman with whom she discusses casework.*

**Provenance.** Via Cage's social orbit (Heroes for Hire / Daughters of the Dragon overlap). Friendship is functional first, warm second.

**Architecture.** Two women who run private investigation / bail / recovery work in adjacent ecosystems. They share intelligence, refer cases, and have a documented mutual-aid protocol around childcare for Danielle (Misty is, on file, the named first-call backstop if Cage is offline). Their banter is shorter and drier than the Carol or Drew registers — less affect, more competence.

**Trust / safety.** High-operational, medium-emotional. Misty is one of the few people subject will accept tactical correction from without the correction triggering Killgrave-coded resistance, because Misty's authority is earned-in-domain rather than positional.

**Failure mode.** Geographic and bandwidth limits; they go months without contact and the relationship survives this without strain, which is itself diagnostic of its health.

**Leverage.** Moderate-operational; low coercive.

---

## 11. J. JONAH JAMESON — *Boss; nemesis-shaped mentor; the institutional figure she could not fully despise.*

**Provenance.** Hires her at the Daily Bugle to cover the superhuman beat during *Pulse*. He is, characteristically, exploitative, loud, and indifferent to her wellbeing in the surface register — and yet the relationship contains data that resist the easy reading.

**Architecture.** Jameson is the only institutional authority figure in the file who has ever paid her a regular check and whose direct interpersonal contempt she did not internalize. The mechanism is specific: Jameson's contempt is universal, theatrical, and unrelated to her — therefore safe. He yells at her the way he yells at Parker, at Robertson, at the wall. She is not singled out. For someone whose central wound is being individually targeted by a powerful man, the experience of being non-individually berated by a powerful man is, paradoxically, neutral or even relieving.

**Evolution.** Antagonistic-functional → grudging mutual respect → eventual exit on civil terms.

**Trust / safety.** Medium-low emotional, medium operational. She would not confide; she would, at need, accept his help, and has.

**Failure mode.** His sensationalism repeatedly endangers her sources and the people she covers; she has compensated by lying to him about leads.

**Leverage.** Low-moderate; institutional access remains a residual asset she could reactivate.

---

## CROSS-CUTTING NOTES

**Vetting protocol (inferred).** Subject runs new entrants through a consistent gauntlet: initial profanity-saturated cold front (filters out the thin-skinned); a deliberate display of unattractive behavior, often drinking-related (filters out the moralistic and the rescuers); a small operational ask that requires the candidate to act before being thanked (filters out reciprocity-trackers). Survivors of all three are eligible for inclusion. The protocol has not meaningfully changed in fifteen years of publication.

**Disclosure topology.** Information flows asymmetrically across the circle. Cage receives the most, in delay. Carol receives historical material Cage does not. Murdock receives ethical/legal material no one else does. Malcolm receives operational material in real time and emotional material almost never. Drew receives bodily-violation register material no one else can metabolize. Danielle receives presence. No single member holds the full picture, and this is by design — it is the firewalling discussed above, and it is the structural legacy of having once been wholly known and wholly controlled by Killgrave.

**The absent category.** Subject has no therapist, no sponsor, no clergy, no formal mentor, and no peer support group on file. The chosen-family circle is doing the work that, for a less self-reliant survivor, would be distributed across professional infrastructure. This is sustainable while the circle holds. It is a single point of failure if it doesn't.

**Operational summary for the file.** To predict subject, model the circle. To support subject, support Cage. To threaten subject, threaten Danielle — and accept that you will not survive the response.

*End RELATIONSHIPS II.*


## Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

# RELATIONSHIPS III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

*Compiled: every figure with whom the Subject has stood in opposition, competition, or sexual/romantic entanglement. Cross-reference with Relationships I (Family/Chosen) and Relationships II (Professional/Network). Note: in the Subject's life these categories braid — several of her rivals are former lovers, several of her enemies were once colleagues, and one enemy retains involuntary tenancy in her interior life.*

---

## I. ENEMIES

### 1. Zebediah Killgrave — "The Purple Man"

**Status:** Living. Periodically incarcerated (the Raft; various enhanced-detention facilities). Functionally, in the Subject's interior architecture, never absent.

**History.** Eight months of captivity in the Subject's early twenties. The mechanism — pheromonally enforced compliance — distinguishes this relationship from any other category of victimization in her file. Killgrave did not, in his own framing, "do" things to her; he caused her to do them, watch them, beg for them. The phenomenological cruelty is that the Subject retains memories indexed as *her own actions and wants*, not as things done to her body. She has spent two decades trying to re-author those memories as coercion without entirely succeeding — the residual self-blame is the wound that refuses to close.

He resurfaces in *Alias* #24–28 (the "Purple" arc), gets free again during the *New Avengers* era, sires the Purple Children whose existence triggers the 2016 *Jessica Jones* arc, and remains the gravitational center of her case-selection bias: she takes Killgrave-adjacent cases at a rate far above chance, partly because she is the foremost expert on him and partly because she cannot resist the proximity.

**Dynamics.** Killgrave's relationship to the Subject is one of *connoisseurship*. He regards her as his finest work — the only victim with the strength to have hurt him, the only one who got away "still capable," the canvas on which his cruelty achieved its highest fidelity. He flirts, in his confrontations with her, with paternal pride. This is more unbearable to her than hatred would be.

The Subject's relationship to Killgrave is asymmetric. He is the only enemy who can make her stutter, freeze mid-sentence, or vomit (post-confrontation, *Alias*). Voice alone — even his recorded voice, even his phrasings reproduced by others — produces a cascade response: visual narrowing, hand tremor, breath-holding, then suppressive overcontrol. She has trained herself to defeat this in the moment of operational need and to pay the somatic cost privately, afterward, in the office, with whiskey.

**Trust/safety.** Zero, asymptotic. She does not believe he can be cured, contained, killed, or escaped from. The Jean Grey psychic block is the only thing that allowed the *Alias* finale to function; the Subject treats it as load-bearing infrastructure and any rumor of its degradation triggers preparatory behaviors (will-drafting in implication, increased perimeter awareness around Danielle's daycare, sleep collapse).

**Failure mode.** When Killgrave-coded stimuli appear, the Subject becomes uncharacteristically predictable. She will go alone. She will not tell Cage. She will not call Carol. She will not call Murdock. The 2016 *Jessica Jones* arc is the cardinal documentation: presented with a Purple-Children-related threat, she fabricates a marital separation, allows her child to be removed from her custody, accepts incarceration, and severs all close ties — all to deny Killgrave any hostage-leverage. Operationally elegant. Relationally devastating. She would do it again identically.

**Leverage (his over her).** Voice. The color purple in any saturated quantity in unexpected context. Any third party speaking in commands of the form "you will / you want to." The mere word "please" in certain cadences. Threats to anyone she loves framed as *her fault* (this is his preferred attack vector, and the only one to which she has no rehearsed defense).

**Leverage (hers over him).** She has hurt him physically — she is one of vanishingly few people who has landed a punch that registered on his face. He fears, and is fascinated by, this. Operationally, in confrontation, *affect denial* is her weapon: refusing the reaction he came to harvest. She has gotten better at this with reps.

---

### 2. The Purple Children

**Status:** Several, scattered, ages varying. The Subject is unwilling to enumerate them precisely; she keeps a list, encrypted, in a notebook she does not back up.

**History.** Killgrave's biological offspring, inheriting variations of his power. Their existence implicates her — not because she is their mother, but because she is, in her own moral accounting, *responsible for monsters Killgrave has made when she was supposed to have stopped him*.

**Dynamics.** She cannot hate them. Several are children; some are victims of their own father; one or more have used powers against her. Her response is bifurcated: tactical wariness layered over a quasi-parental ache she will not name aloud. She has, on at least one occasion, protected a Purple Child from adults who wished it dead, at significant cost to her credibility.

**Trust/safety.** Provisional, case-by-case, structurally unstable. She assumes Killgrave can reach them. She assumes they can reach her.

**Failure mode.** She will accept compulsion from a Purple Child she believes is acting in good faith before she will accept it from any other source — and she should not.

**Leverage.** Any of them who develop their father's command capacity inherit, by default, all of his tools against her.

---

### 3. The Avengers (as an institutional adversary, *Alias*-era)

**Status:** Reconciled in part, never wholly forgiven in the limbic record.

**History.** The cardinal injury, second only to Killgrave himself: after she attacked Ms. Marvel under Killgrave's compulsion, the Vision and Iron Man beat her into a coma. No Avenger visited the hospital. No Avenger learned, contemporaneously, that she had been mind-controlled. The institution treated her as a defeated villain and then forgot her. She woke alone.

**Dynamics.** The Subject's lifelong contempt for "capes" originates here, not in adolescent cynicism. The contempt is precisely calibrated to the institution's failure mode: ostentatious heroism, poor follow-through on the people they hurt incidentally. Her later residence at Avengers Tower (post-Cage, post-marriage) is conducted in the spirit of a witness inside enemy walls; she sleeps lightly there for years.

**Trust/safety.** Individually variable (see Carol Danvers, below); institutionally low. She will work with Avengers and never *of* them.

**Failure mode.** When the Avengers as a body convene a decision about her or her family, she will withdraw rather than advocate, because she has tested whether they will hear her once and the answer was no.

**Leverage.** They have institutional resources she occasionally needs. She has, in turn, leverage on them as the surviving witness to their worst pattern: she is what their negligence looks like, walking around, working.

---

### 4. J. Jonah Jameson

**Status:** Former employer (*The Pulse*), perpetual irritant, complicated semi-affection.

**History.** Hires her at the Bugle when she is pregnant and brittle, ostensibly for her expertise on superhuman affairs, actually for the Spider-Man column he hopes she will eventually write. He underpays her, shouts at her, and prints things she opposes. She, in turn, leaks against him quietly, refuses to write what he most wants, and continues to take his paychecks longer than she should.

**Dynamics.** A mutually contemptuous functional alliance. Jameson respects competence and she has it; she respects directness and he has that, even when the direction is wrong. He never patronizes her about her trauma — partly because he doesn't fully know about it, partly because patronizing is not in his repertoire toward subordinates.

**Trust/safety.** Low-trust, high-predictability. She knows exactly how he will behave, which makes him paradoxically restful to be around.

**Failure mode.** She will tolerate his abuse longer than her self-respect should permit because he is a known quantity in an unknown world.

**Leverage.** Each holds the other's professional reputation in a glass jar and neither will throw.

---

### 5. Norman Osborn (Dark Reign era)

**Status:** Active enemy during the *Siege* / Dark Reign window; afterward a figure she watches the horizon for.

**History.** During Osborn's ascendancy, the Subject and Cage relocate Danielle multiple times, change apartments, harden communication. The Subject — operating with a small child — is forced to treat the federal government as a hostile actor. This activates every Killgrave-era schema (powerful man with institutional cover, weaponizing legitimacy).

**Dynamics.** Pure threat-assessment. She has no relationship to Osborn personally; she relates to him as a category — *the abuser who has acquired a flag*.

**Trust/safety.** None.

**Failure mode.** Her response to Osborn-class threats is to flee with the child first and explain to allies afterward, which has cost her relational capital with people who would have helped.

**Leverage.** None direct. Indirect: Cage's standing among the New Avengers and the Heroes for Hire network is the perimeter she relies on.

---

### 6. The Skrull Posing as Jarvis ("Skrull-Jarvis")

**Status:** Dead/exposed (*Secret Invasion*). Active in the Subject's nightmares.

**History.** The infiltrator who took Danielle from Avengers Tower while wearing the face the Subject had trusted to babysit. The breach is structurally identical to her core wound: she gave her will (in the form of her child) to a figure she believed safe, and that figure was an impostor exploiting the form of safety.

**Dynamics.** N/A — adversary is dead. Pattern persists.

**Trust/safety.** The injury generalized. After Skrull-Jarvis the Subject's threshold for accepting childcare from anyone non-superhuman collapsed; her solution (Squirrel Girl) is widely mocked as comic but is rigorously logical — a nanny who could in principle defeat most credible threats and who is constitutionally incapable of the kind of long-game infiltration Skrull-Jarvis represented.

**Failure mode.** Any caretaker whose identity she cannot verify on metaphysical grounds is, to her, presumptively suspect.

**Leverage.** None remaining.

---

## II. RIVALS

The Subject's rivalries are less competitive than they are *frictions of comparison* — relationships with peers whose existence raises questions she does not wish to answer about her own life.

### 1. Carol Danvers (when oppositional)

*(Carol's full mapping appears under Chosen Family. Here only the rivalrous register.)*

**Dynamics.** The Subject attacked Carol under Killgrave's compulsion. Carol — characteristically — forgave her early, befriended her hard, and has spent two decades being the one friend the Subject cannot quite metabolize. The rivalrous component is not jealousy of Carol's power or status; it is the unbearable comparison of *trajectories*. Carol weathered comparable losses by leaning into the cape identity; the Subject weathered hers by repudiating it. Each implies a judgment of the other.

**Failure mode.** When Carol arrives in full Captain Marvel mode — uniformed, command-cadenced, sincere — the Subject hears, faintly, Killgrave-tonality in any imperative phrasing, and reacts disproportionately. The 2016 arc's rupture with Carol is the most documented instance: the Subject's withdrawal reads to Carol as betrayal and reads to the Subject as protection.

**Leverage.** Each could destroy the other's standing with a sentence; neither will. The mutual restraint is the architecture of the friendship.
