---

### 2. Spider-Man / Peter Parker

**Status:** Old classmate, occasional collaborator, never-named-aloud lifelong comparator.

**History.** They attended Midtown High simultaneously. He was, in her adolescent perception, *the one who was nothing and became something* — the inverse of her own trajectory, which she narrates as *the one who was something and was broken by it.* Her adolescent fixation on him (per *Alias* flashbacks) was envious, hostile, and never resolved into a real interpersonal relationship. As adults they have crossed paths professionally and respect each other warily.

**Dynamics.** She cannot quite forgive him for being the version of post-trauma superheroism that worked. He cannot quite figure out why she does not like him more.

**Trust/safety.** Operationally high (she would trust him with Danielle in a pinch). Emotionally low (she will not be vulnerable in his presence).

**Failure mode.** She becomes more sarcastic, more guarded, and worse at her job in his immediate vicinity.

**Leverage.** Mutual knowledge of each other's secret identities, neither weaponized.

---

### 3. Mattie Franklin (Spider-Woman)

**Status:** Former case subject, complicated. (Status fluctuates with continuity; treat as a fixed presence in the file regardless.)

**History.** The *Alias* MGH case — Mattie, addicted, pimped out for her powers, rescued by the Subject in one of the bleakest sequences in the run. The case is foundational to the Subject's professional identity: it was the first time she understood that her PI work was not a flight from heroism but a more precise form of it.

**Dynamics.** Not rivalrous in the conventional sense. The friction is that Mattie represents *what the Subject could have become* under slightly different circumstances — a younger woman crushed by the cape world she was too unequipped to enter.

**Trust/safety.** Protective, parental, asymmetric.

**Failure mode.** The Subject over-identifies and over-extends.

**Leverage.** None either direction.

---

### 4. Other PIs and "street-level" operators

A diffuse category — Misty Knight, Colleen Wing, Dakota North, occasional bail bondsmen and ex-cops in her professional ecosystem. The Subject's rivalry posture here is *competence-comparative*: she watches them work, notes what they do better, refuses to ask for tutelage, occasionally borrows methods without attribution. Misty in particular is treated with wary respect; the Subject regards her as the version of street-investigator who has not had to drink her way through it, and the comparison is unflattering enough that the Subject keeps the relationship at handshake distance.

---

## III. LOVERS

The Subject's sexual history divides cleanly into three periods: pre-Killgrave (sparse, age-appropriate, unremarkable in the file); *Alias*-era (compulsive, dissociative, self-punishing); post-Cage (monogamous, fraught, real). The lovers themselves are few. The bodies in beds were more numerous than the relationships; the file should be honest about both.

### 1. Luke Cage

**Status:** Husband. Father of Danielle. Co-parent. Co-resident. The relationship of her adult life.

**History.** First encounter: a one-night stand during her brief Knightress period, post-rooftop fight, transactional and unsentimental on both sides. Second encounter (*Alias* #1): she goes to his bar drunk, propositions him, leaves the next morning unable to face what happened. He, characteristically, does not make her face it; he files it as a thing two adults did and waits to see what she does next. What she does next, over the following two years of *Alias*, is circle him at a widening distance, then narrow it, then accept his persistent low-pressure availability as a kind of structural fact she can lean on.

The pregnancy precedes the marriage; the marriage precedes the full domesticity; the domesticity precedes the open acknowledgment that this is the central relationship of her life. She does each thing in approximately the wrong order and Cage absorbs each delay without comment.

**Dynamics.** Cage's most operationally relevant trait, for her, is *invariance*. He does not change his affect under stress, does not change his commitment under provocation, does not change his presence under her withdrawal. After Killgrave, after the Avengers' negligence, after every relational schema in her file teaching her that proximity is provisional, Cage represents a sustained empirical refutation. She tests this constantly — picks fights, withdraws sex, disappears for days during cases, says cruel things calibrated to provoke a retaliatory withdrawal that would confirm her schema. He does not provide it. The slow erosion of her certainty that he will leave is the central emotional work of her life past forty.

Sexually, the relationship is the only documented one in her file that approximates embodied presence. She is not, per implication, fully present every time; she still dissociates under certain configurations; but the *baseline* is participated rather than endured. This is non-trivial.

Verbal style between them: terse, profane, mutually mocking, affectionate by inference. They do not speak in the registers of romantic disclosure. Direct expressions of love are rare and conducted at oblique angles, often in third-person reference to Danielle. Each understands the other's coding without translation.

**Trust/safety.** The highest in her file. Not absolute — she still withholds (the 2016 arc is the cardinal failure) — but the baseline trust is structurally different from any other relationship's. He is the one person she will permit to *advise* her without it triggering Killgrave-coded compulsion sensitivity, because his advisory cadence is built explicitly around her sensitivity.

**Failure mode.** When she is most overwhelmed — Killgrave-coded threats, threats to Danielle — she will lie to Cage *to protect him from her decisions*. This is the form her love takes under maximum stress, and it is the form most destructive to the relationship. The 2016 arc documents this in full: she sells Cage a fake separation so completely that he believes it, and the marital damage is real even after the truth comes out. Her capacity to lie convincingly to him is also, paradoxically, evidence of how well she knows him.

The secondary failure mode is alcohol-mediated affect leakage — she will say things drunk she would not say sober, and Cage will categorize them correctly as alcohol-speaking, which is generous of him but does not entirely defuse the damage.

**Leverage.** Each holds total leverage on the other and neither has ever invoked it. The marriage is built on the mutual non-exercise of available power. Danielle, as a person, is not leverage between them — she is the joint project that has reorganized both their lives around something other than themselves.

---

### 2. Scott Lang (failed dating attempt, *Alias*-era)

**Status:** Briefly attempted dating; never consummated as a relationship; cordial residuum.

**History.** Scott — recently widowed, gentle, awkwardly available — attempts to date the Subject during the *Alias* run. She accepts the attempts in principle and sabotages them in practice. The arc is short and the file's documentation of it is mostly her interior commentary about why a decent man trying to be decent with her is unbearable.

**Dynamics.** Scott's gentleness is precisely the thing she cannot tolerate at that period of her life. Cage's flat-affect tolerance is workable because it does not require her to be tender in return. Scott's tenderness asks her, implicitly, to be tender, and she has nothing to give back; the deficit shames her; the shame becomes contempt; the contempt becomes the small cruelties that end the attempt.

**Trust/safety.** Trust was offered and not received. Safety, ironically, would have been high — Scott is one of the safest men in her ecosystem.

**Failure mode.** She drove a fundamentally sound prospect off through preemptive devaluation. She knows it. It is one of the *Alias*-era data points she revisits when assessing how much Killgrave cost her in capacity to receive ordinary kindness.

**Leverage.** None either direction. They are cordial when they cross.

---

### 3. Anonymous *Alias*-era sexual partners

A category, not individuals. The file should record the pattern rather than the names — which, in many cases, the Subject herself does not retain.

**Pattern.** Bar pickups, late-night, alcohol-mediated, often with men she actively disliked. The selection criterion was *unsuitability* — choosing partners she could not be expected to want, in order to ensure the encounter remained a transaction rather than a connection. Mornings were characterized by accelerated departure, occasional vomiting, no exchange of contact information. The encounters did not involve the use of her powers; she was scrupulous about not putting partners in physical risk, which is its own data point about her moral floor under intoxication.

**Function.** Self-punishment indistinguishable, in her own retrospect, from affect regulation. Sex as somatic discharge, with the partner as instrument. She has spoken of this period in flashback with the same retrospective revulsion she applies to the Jewel costume — both are versions of herself she encapsulates rather than integrates.

**Trust/safety.** Operationally low, not because of partner-risk (she could defeat any of them) but because of the recklessness about screening and the elevated risk of running into a Killgrave-coded scenario while compromised. She got lucky. She knows it.

**Failure mode.** The pattern would have killed someone — her, or eventually a partner she misjudged — if the Cage relationship had not interrupted it.

**Leverage.** None. Most of these men would not recognize her now.

---

### 4. Pre-Killgrave / Jewel-era encounters

Sparse documentation; one or two implied relationships of an age-appropriate, low-intensity character. The file notes them for completeness and for the contrast they provide: the Subject was, before Killgrave, capable of ordinary embodied affection at age-appropriate stakes. The capacity was not absent in her; it was extracted. Whether it has been fully restored, or only approximated through the specific architecture of the Cage marriage, is the central open question of her interior life and the question this dossier — committed to honesty rather than reassurance — must leave open.

---

## IV. CROSS-CATEGORY NOTES

**The braid.** Cage is the only figure who appears in no other relational category — he is solely a lover/partner, never a rival, never an enemy. Every other intimate has, at some point, been one of the others: Carol has been both ally and oppositional force; Mattie has been both case-subject and protégée-rival; even Killgrave occupies, in the Subject's most disturbed moments, a relational slot that has nothing to do with enmity and everything to do with the perverse intimacy of long captivity. This braiding is itself diagnostic — the Subject does not maintain clean categorical separations between people who help her and people who hurt her, because her foundational experience taught her that the categories collapse.

**The pattern of leverage non-exercise.** Across every meaningful relationship in this section, the Subject holds significant leverage and does not use it. This is not weakness; it is the operative form of her moral floor. She knows what it is to have one's will instrumentalized. She will not do it to others, even adversaries, except in the narrow case of operational necessity against a credible threat. This restraint, sustained across decades, is the single strongest argument that Killgrave did not, in the end, get what he came for.

*End of section. Cross-reference: Relationships I (Family/Chosen), Relationships II (Professional Network), Behavioral Patterns IV (Substance & Sexuality), Threat Assessment II (Killgrave Protocols).*


## Powers, Abilities & Their Limits

# POWERS, ABILITIES & THEIR LIMITS

## 1. Inventory of Capacities

Jessica Jones is, in the strict technical sense, a meta-empowered individual at roughly the lower-middle tier of the Marvel street/Avengers gradient — measurably superhuman, nowhere near apex. The capacities, ordered by reliability:

**Strength.** Class 25 baseline with episodic surges into Class 50+ under adrenaline. Canonically she has dented quinjet hull plating, thrown a sedan one-handed across a parking lot (*Alias* #25), traded blows with a Power Man who outranks her by roughly a factor of two, and casually torn a steel security door off its hinges in the Owl arc of *The Pulse*. She is stronger than Spider-Man on a pound-for-pound basis but slower and less precise. She does not know her own ceiling because she has never tested it — a critical, recurring point.

**Durability.** Sub-Cage and clearly under She-Hulk. She can absorb blunt-force trauma that would kill a baseline human (falls from altitude, Sentry-tier shockwaves at distance, point-blank handgun rounds bruise rather than penetrate at close range). She is NOT bulletproof in the Cage sense — high-caliber rounds, edged weapons, and superhuman strikes injure her. The Vision/Iron Man beating that put her in a coma is the empirical proof of her ceiling: sustained engagement by Avengers-tier opposition will break her.

**Flight.** Present but degraded. Self-described in *Alias* #22 as "controlled falling" and "jumping really high and hoping." Mechanism appears to be a low-power gravitic or kinetic field she cannot finely modulate. She can ascend, hover briefly, and descend with rough directional control; she cannot maneuver in three dimensions with confidence, cannot accelerate cleanly, and reports nausea on extended use. She avoids flight in public almost categorically.

**Resilience adjuncts.** Accelerated recovery (not a healing factor — she does not regenerate, but bruises and lacerations resolve over days rather than weeks). Reduced need for sleep, which she squanders by drinking through it rather than benefiting from it. Implied minor resistance to toxins and intoxicants, contradicted in practice by the fact that she gets drunk constantly — meaning either the resistance is modest, or she compensates by sheer volume.

**Psychic shielding.** Installed by Jean Grey/the X-Men post-coma, specifically against Killgrave's pheromonal compulsion. Effective but imperfect; in the *Alias* "Purple" arc she still has to **decide** to resist in his physical presence, the shield buying her the milliseconds of agency rather than blanket immunity. No general telepathic resistance has been demonstrated.

## 2. Mechanism

The origin substance is the IGH-class experimental compound from the convoy crash. On the available evidence the agent acted on her endocrine and musculoskeletal architecture, not her neurology — she has no enhanced senses, no reflex amplification, no perceptual acceleration. This matters operationally: her body became super, her **nervous system did not**. She fights at human reaction speed with superhuman output, which produces the characteristic Jessica-Jones combat signature: too much force, slightly too late, applied in too straight a line. She breaks things she meant to grip and misses things she meant to hit.

The flight is the most poorly understood component, by her and by canon. The best inference is that it is a secondary expression of the same kinetic enhancement — a coarse field effect she has never learned to refine because she stopped flying before adult practice could discipline it. The Jewel period was when she was still developing it; Killgrave was when she stopped.

## 3. Growth Curve Across Two Decades of Canon

**Adolescent / Jewel phase (powers ascendant).** Active practice. She flies, she patrols, she trains in the loose autodidact way 90s Marvel teen heroes do. Strength and flight both **improve** through use; durability is untested because she avoids serious opposition. This is the only period in which her power profile is on a growth trajectory.

**Captivity (powers neutralized).** Killgrave does not need to suppress her physically; he owns her will. The powers are present but inverted — used **against** her allies, against herself, against strangers. She experiences her own strength as a weapon held to her own head.

**Post-coma / Knightress (powers as tool, identity refused).** She fights, but she stops flying. She stops training. She uses strength as needed and lets the rest atrophy. This is the inflection point: her power profile begins a long, slow **regression toward floor-level** because she will not practice.

**Alias period (powers as embarrassment).** She uses strength reflexively — opening stuck windows, intimidating skips, occasionally hurling a perp into a dumpster — but treats every visible use as a small failure of cover. Flight: essentially zero. Combat: avoidant; she'll take a beating rather than escalate to capes-tier engagement. The powers are, in this period, **suppressed by her relationship to them** more than by any external limit.

**Pulse / pregnancy.** She refuses dangerous engagement on Danielle's behalf; the powers go further into reserve. Pregnancy depicted as physically uneventful — the enhancement appears not to have complicated gestation, but she catastrophizes about it constantly.

**New Avengers / late period.** Forced re-engagement. She fights alongside teammates who out-class her and learns, slowly, to function in that bracket without grandiosity. She still does not fly. She still does not train. She uses what she has, accepts the gap, and compensates with stubbornness and case-knowledge.

**Net trajectory:** a young hero whose raw capacity should have grown over twenty years has instead **plateaued and partially regressed**, with the gap filled by craft, cunning, and pain tolerance. She is, today, less powerful than her potential and more effective than her power profile predicts.

## 4. Non-Powered Skill Stack

The investigative skill set is where two decades of actual practice live, and it is substantial.

- **Surveillance craft.** Long-lens photography (the camera in the *Alias* #1 cold open is a working tool, used competently); static stakeouts with the patience metric of someone who can sit in a cold car for nine hours; tail-work on foot in dense Manhattan traffic with the New Yorker's intuition for sightlines.
- **Document and records work.** She can run a paper trace — court filings, property records, marriage licenses, news morgues. In the Rick Jones case (*Alias* #11–15) she reconstructs an identity-theft network from public-record breadcrumbs.
- **Interview technique.** Two modes: the abrasive mode (deliberate, used to provoke disclosure from defensive subjects) and the quiet mode (rare, used with victims and children — see her handling of Mattie Franklin's mother). She knows which to deploy and when.
- **Street network.** Bartenders, doormen, beat cops who tolerate her, a handful of capes who'll take her call (Murdock, Carol, Cage, Danny Rand, Misty Knight). The network is small, vetted, and reciprocal.
- **Legal literacy.** Working knowledge of New York PI licensure, evidentiary chain-of-custody, what a Murdock-grade attorney can use and what they can't. She runs cases as if anticipating courtroom presentation.
- **Journalism.** Acquired at the *Bugle* under duress; she can write to deadline, source a piece, and survive a Jameson edit, which is its own combat discipline.
- **Combat.** Untrained in any formal sense. No martial art. Her fight style is brawling — a tall woman with super-strength who has been hit a lot and is unafraid of being hit again. She throws hooks, she tackles, she grapples. She is shockingly effective against normal-human opposition and outclassed by anyone with actual technique.
- **Tactical parenting.** A skill, and a hard-won one. The Squirrel Girl nanny decision is its signature application: an apparently absurd choice that is, on inspection, the most defensible babysitter selection in the five boroughs.

## 5. Signature Applications

The recognizable Jessica-Jones operational moves, the ones a sufficiently long file would index:

- **The doorframe entry.** She doesn't kick doors; she shoulders them, low and angled, using mass and strength to pop the latch rather than splinter the frame. Quieter, less destructive, harder to trace.
- **The escalation refusal.** Confronted with super-powered opposition she could plausibly fight, she will routinely choose **not to**, exfiltrating with the client rather than engaging. Read as cowardice by some teammates; is actually a disciplined assessment of her real combat ceiling.
- **The terminal one-liner.** Sarcasm deployed at the climax of an encounter to deny the opponent dignity. Function is partly tactical (provokes mistakes) and partly self-administered — she is reminding herself she is not in a costume.
- **The phone hang-up.** She ends conversations by hanging up. Not dramatically; just abruptly. Operational equivalent of a fortified retreat.
- **The flask in the desk drawer.** Bottom right. The third drawer, by *Pulse*-era panels. Whiskey, not bourbon — she prefers cheaper, harsher, the punishment audible in the choice.
- **The Killgrave protocol.** When Purple-Man-adjacent stimuli surface, she goes silent, fast, alone. She does not call Cage. She does not call Carol. She handles it and tells them after, or doesn't. The 2016 arc is the macro-scale version of this micro-tic.

## 6. Limits — Hard and Self-Imposed

**Hard limits.** No regeneration. No enhanced senses. No formal training. No flight competence. No bullet-proofing at rifle calibers. Combat reaction time at baseline human. Killgrave-class compulsion still requires conscious resistance, not passive immunity. She has been put in comas twice, which is twice more than most Avengers-tier operators.

**Self-imposed limits, which are larger than the hard ones.** She does not fly. She does not train. She does not test her ceiling. She does not wear a costume. She does not use the name Jewel. She does not let the public see her in superhuman mode if avoidable. She does not call for backup until late, often too late. She does not, structurally, **let herself be powerful**, because the last time she felt powerful she was a young woman in a blue-and-white leotard about to lose eight months of her life.

## 7. Symptom-as-Signature

The power profile, read as a clinical exhibit, is the trauma's silhouette.

The **refusal to fly** is the most legible symbol: Killgrave summoned her by air, she came to him by air, she was Jewel in the air. To leave the ground is to re-enter the body-state of the captivity. She walks, she takes the subway, she drives a beat sedan — every modality is a small assertion that her body obeys gravity and her, not him.

The **refusal to train** is the survivor's bargain: if she does not develop the capacity, she cannot be made to deploy it. An untrained super-strong woman who drinks too much is harder to weaponize than a disciplined one. Her atrophy is a defense.

The **suppressed ceiling** — not knowing her own limit — is the converse of grandiose compensation. A narcissistic survivor would inflate the powers into identity. She does the opposite: she keeps them under-measured so they cannot become a self-concept she'd have to defend.

The **investigative craft as substitute capacity** is the sublimation in textbook form: hypervigilance that would otherwise rot inward becomes professional observation; mistrust becomes evidentiary discipline; the inability to take anyone's word becomes a methodology. The *Alias* office is, structurally, a PTSD symptom that pays rent.

The **combat brawling style** — too much force, slightly late, straight-line — is the somatic record of an enhancement laid over an untrained nervous system by a woman who never wanted to be enhanced. She fights the way she lives: with more power than she's comfortable having, deployed reluctantly, accepting the collateral damage as the price of getting it over with.

And the **psychic shield**, the one piece of her power profile installed by someone else, is the only component that protects her without requiring her to act. It is also the one she most resents needing — proof, in her own architecture, that she could not save herself alone. She has, characteristically, never thanked Jean Grey for it on panel.

Her power, in sum, is a body she did not ask for, deployed by a will that has been violated and has organized everything since around not being violated again. The capacities are real, measurable, and consistently under-used. The skills are unglamorous, hand-built, and consistently under-credited. What she has, she has earned by refusing to be what was done to her — and the shape of that refusal is the shape of every power she has and every power she will not use.


## Operational Profile & Tradecraft

# OPERATIONAL PROFILE & TRADECRAFT

## Jessica Campbell Jones — Alias Investigations
*Section 6 of UATU Dossier. Cross-reference §3 (Psychological) and §5 (Personality) for motivational substrate; this section addresses MECHANICS.*

---

## 6.1 Decision-Making Architecture

Subject's decision process is best modeled as a **three-layer stack** that runs in a fixed order under almost all conditions, with the layers communicating poorly across the boundary.

**Layer 1 — Threat triage (fast, somatic, ~200ms).** A pre-cognitive scan trained by Killgrave captivity and a decade of street investigation. Subject reads a room for: exits, the largest male, anyone whose eye-line lingers, voice-cadence anomalies (a Killgrave-trained tell — she listens for the imperative register), purple in the visual field, anyone whose affect doesn't match their words. This layer fires constantly and is the source of her chronic baseline fatigue. She does not turn it off; she dampens it with alcohol.

**Layer 2 — Case logic (deliberate, verbal, often pen-on-legal-pad).** Surprisingly conventional gumshoe reasoning: who benefits, who was last to see, who is lying about which specific thing. She is methodical, builds timelines, works the phone hard. She is NOT a forensic technician, NOT a hacker, NOT a fighter-first investigator. She solves cases by being the person willing to make the eighteenth phone call and ask the rude question.

**Layer 3 — Personal/relational decisions (slow, conflicted, often deferred via drinking).** This is where her process breaks. Decisions involving Cage, Danielle, Carol, Malcolm, or her own future are routinely postponed by displacement activity (taking an extra case, getting drunk, picking a fight to change the subject). When forced, she defaults to the **unilateral covert option** — the *Jessica Jones* 2016 arc is the canonical example: rather than enlist her husband, her closest friend (Carol Danvers), or any institutional ally, she fakes a separation, accepts incarceration, alienates everyone, and runs the op alone. This is not tactical preference; it is a trauma-derived axiom (*proximity = leverage against me*) operating as if it were strategy.

**Decision speed.** Fast under physical threat (her best moments). Average on cases. Catastrophically slow on anything requiring her to ask for help. She will burn weeks rather than make the call.

---

## 6.2 Casework Method

**Intake.** Walk-ins to the Alias office (a two-room rental in lower Manhattan, ground-floor or low-floor, frosted-glass door reading ALIAS INVESTIGATIONS in chipped vinyl lettering, no reception, no assistant in the *Alias* era — Malcolm Ducasse later occupies a desk in the front room and handles screening). Initial client conversation conducted from behind the desk, leaning back, feet often on a drawer, cigarette or coffee in hand. She offers no water, no comfort cues. **Tell:** she lets silences run long — clients fill them with the actual story. This is deliberate; she learned it works.

**Fee structure.** Cash preferred. Standard private-investigator day rates with expenses; she undercuts the going Manhattan rate, which is one reason cash-flow is perpetually marginal. She accepts payment-in-kind from people who can't pay (a recurring trait — favors banked rather than refused). Discount for victims; full freight plus markup for spouses of cheaters with money. She will refuse a case if the client reads as the predator rather than the prey — sometimes loudly.

**Case selection bias.** Disproportionately takes missing-persons (especially missing women and missing teens), domestic-fear cases, and cases that smell wrong on the phone. Avoids: corporate work, divorce-financial work that doesn't involve abuse, anything that requires courtroom testimony she'd have to give sober and on the record about herself. **The strong filter:** she takes the case if the client is structurally weaker than the target. This is the **Drive #1** of §5 executed as a screening criterion.

**Investigative tradecraft (street level).**
- **Surveillance:** old-school. Sits in a parked car (whichever beater is currently running) with coffee and a paperback for cover, sometimes for ten-hour stretches. Bathroom-discipline poor (relies on the diner across whatever street she's on; favors diners specifically because they tolerate one-coffee occupation).
- **Pretexting:** excellent. Her natural affect — surly, profane, working-class New York — is wildly disarming to people who expect a PI to be slick. She'll claim to be a process server, a city inspector, a sister of the missing, a journalist. Voice doesn't change much; cover story is delivered with the same flat affect, which paradoxically reads as authentic.
- **Document work:** competent but unglamorous. Public records, court filings, property records — she knows the clerks at several Manhattan and Queens offices by first name and tips them at Christmas (a quiet, consistent expense). She does not forge documents herself; she knows who does.
- **Digital tradecraft:** marginal and dated. She types with two fingers, uses search engines and email, has a basic understanding of social-media reconnaissance, but defers complex digital work to others (Malcolm increasingly; in *New Avengers* / *Pulse* era, Bugle staff; occasionally a hacker contact paid in cash). Her phone is whatever cheap model is current; she does not encrypt by default. **This is a real vulnerability** in a Palantir-grade adversarial environment.
- **Photography:** keeps a cheap DSLR or, later, just uses her phone. Photos are evidentiary, not artful. She is not above climbing a fire escape to get the shot.
- **Interrogation:** her signature move. She lets the subject talk past the lie, asks the same question three different ways across the conversation, and watches the answer drift. Will use her strength as implicit threat — picking something up, denting a doorframe casually — but rarely as explicit violence in interview. The exception: predators of children or sexual abusers, where she abandons all restraint and the interview becomes coercion.

**Force tradecraft.** She fights like someone who is much stronger than her opponent and knows it but does not enjoy it. Closes distance, grabs, throws, hits once hard. No martial discipline — Cage has tried, Danvers has tried, she resists structured training because structure-imposed-on-her-body is a Killgrave-coded trigger. **Result:** she is dangerous but inefficient, takes more hits than she should, and is genuinely vulnerable to a trained opponent at her power tier. She knows this and avoids fights she could lose by choice of engagement rather than skill.

**Flight tradecraft.** Almost never uses flight tactically. Walks or cabs. The few times she flies in published material, it is under acute duress or for someone else's benefit (carrying Danielle, evacuating a civilian). The avoidance is psychological, not physical: flight = Jewel = Killgrave summoning her. **Operational signature:** if Jones is airborne, the situation has decompensated.

---

## 6.3 Infrastructure

**Office.** Alias Investigations, lower Manhattan, walking distance from a half-dozen bars she rotates through. Furnishings: secondhand desk, filing cabinets that actually contain files (she keeps paper — partly habit, partly correctly distrusting that anyone has subpoenaed her servers), couch (slept on regularly), mini-fridge (beer, expired condiments, occasionally evidence). Door has been replaced multiple times — she's broken it herself, others have broken it coming in. **Security:** minimal. A deadbolt. No alarm in the *Alias* era; eventually a basic system after Danielle is born and the apartment becomes co-located concern. Operationally she relies on the fact that an intruder who is not enhanced is not a meaningful threat to her, and an intruder who IS enhanced will not be stopped by an alarm.

**Residence — *Alias* era.** Small apartment, separate from the office, sparsely furnished, dish-pile in sink, laundry on floor, fridge containing beer and takeout containers. Mattress on a frame, not on the floor (a low bar she clears). Bathroom medicine cabinet: ibuprofen, no prescriptions, drugstore makeup she rarely uses, a single nice perfume bottle she does not open.

**Residence — *Pulse* / *New Avengers* / married era.** Avengers Tower suite during the *New Avengers* run; she is visibly uncomfortable there, uses one room as a fortified nest, leaves the rest under-occupied. Later: shared apartment with Cage and Danielle, materially upgraded (Cage's housekeeping, his discipline), but she retains a personal mess-zone — a desk, a chair, a corner — that she does not let anyone clean.

**Vehicles.** Whichever used sedan is currently running. No registered fleet, no lease — she pays cash, lets registration lapse, parks badly. In *Pulse* she occasionally uses Bugle expensed cars and resents it. Public transit and walking are primary; she knows the New York subway grid at the level of someone who has tailed people on it.

**Wardrobe (operational uniform).** Jeans, T-shirt or thermal, leather jacket (a specific one, worn for years, repaired rather than replaced — the jacket is identity-bearing), boots (combat or motorcycle style, scuffed). Sports bra. Underwear plain. Hair short-to-medium, washed but rarely styled, often air-dried. No jewelry except, in the married period, the wedding ring she sometimes removes and pockets when working. **Cosmetics:** chapstick, occasional eyeliner if she's pretexting at a venue that requires it. The wardrobe is also a **defensive aesthetic**: the inverse of the Jewel costume, by design. To look at her is to be told she is not asking to be looked at.

**Communications.** Cellphone, replaced when broken (frequently). Burner phones for specific cases, bought from bodegas in cash, used and discarded. Maintains an answering-machine / voicemail at the office that she screens. Email account she checks once a day at most. Her digital footprint is small not by sophistication but by neglect — which, accidentally, is good tradecraft.

**Weapons.** None routinely carried. She is the weapon. Will pick up environmental objects (rebar, pipes, car doors) as needed. Has been depicted with a baseball bat in the office — kept by the door, more totem than tool.

---

## 6.4 Finances

**Income.** Volatile. *Alias* era: subsistence-level PI revenue, cash and check, frequently late. *Pulse* era: Daily Bugle salary (modest, with benefits — and the benefits matter; this is when she has structured prenatal care). Post-*Pulse*: returns to Alias Investigations, supplemented by Cage's earnings as a Hero for Hire / Avenger stipends.

**Spending pattern.** Low fixed overhead (cheap office, modest rent). High variable on: alcohol (a real line-item — she drinks at bars, which is more expensive than home-drinking and indicates social need masked as habit), takeout, cabs, cigarette costs in the early period. Negligible on: clothing, furniture, vacations (none depicted), personal grooming, savings.

**Banking.** Has accounts but treats them with suspicion. Keeps a cash reserve in the office (a drawer, a coffee can — typical PI behavior). No depicted investments, no retirement account in evidence, no real-estate ownership pre-Cage.

**Credit and institutional footprint.** Likely poor credit (late payments, sporadic income, no apparent active management). Files taxes — she is too rule-conscious in a specific way to not file — but does so minimally and probably under-reports cash. Carries health insurance only when the Bugle provides it; uninsured for stretches, which is operationally relevant (she will not go to a hospital for injuries she can sleep off, partly cost, partly avoidance of records).

**Vulnerabilities.** Cash-flow stress is a reliable lever — a sufficiently large retainer for a sufficiently ugly case will pull her in further than she'd like. She has, however, **refused money on principle** repeatedly (turning down work from clients who set off the predator-filter), which means the lever is conditional, not absolute.

---

## 6.5 Network and Human Infrastructure

**Inner ring (full trust, will mobilize for).** Luke Cage (spouse, tactical partner, the one person she will sometimes — not always — actually call). Danielle Cage (daughter, organizing principle). Malcolm Ducasse (employee, eventually partner — a recovered Killgrave victim, which makes the relationship structurally unique; he is the only person who knows the wound from the inside). Carol Danvers (oldest friend; relationship periodically ruptured and repaired — the friendship survives because Danvers tolerates the silences).

**Second ring (operational allies, conditional trust).** Matt Murdock (legal cover, occasional case overlap, mutual respect with friction). Jessica Drew (rarer interaction, but present). Misty Knight (PI-to-PI professional regard). Daredevil-circle and Heroes for Hire-circle by extension through Cage.

**Third ring (instruments, not friends).** J. Jonah Jameson (employer, foil, useful idiot in both directions). Various NYPD detectives she has cultivated over years — the bar-coffee-and-favors relationship, no names she'd give to a federal subpoena. Bail bondsmen, document people, a hacker or two, a doctor who does off-books work for capes, a bartender or two who let her run tabs. These are the dark-matter relationships a total-surveillance file would map carefully: they are how she actually gets things done.

**Adversarial / historical.** Killgrave (active threat indefinitely, even when imprisoned — psychological as much as physical). The Purple Children (ambiguous). The original Avengers roster she attacked under compulsion — relationship with them ranges from repaired (Danvers) to permanently strained (institutional Avengers leadership at various points).

**Network hygiene.** Poor by tradecraft standards, sufficient by her actual threat model. She does not compartmentalize her inner ring from her casework — Cage and Malcolm both know what she's working on, usually. She does compartmentalize her **inner state** from everyone, which is the inverse of conventional security culture: she leaks operational details and protects emotional ones, where a trained operator would do the reverse.

---

## 6.6 Daily and Weekly Rhythms

**Sleep.** Late to bed (1–3 a.m. typical), late to rise (9–11 a.m.), fragmented. On a case: sleeps at the office on the couch, three to five hours, wakes to whatever woke her. With Danielle in the household: forced earlier rise, partial adaptation, chronic sleep debt.

**Morning ritual.** Coffee (black, large, from a specific bodega or diner — she is a creature of route). Cigarette in the *Alias* era; quit during pregnancy, occasional relapse under stress. Reads the paper (literally, paper) — Bugle and Times, scanning the metro and crime sections for case-relevant items and for her own name.

**Workday.** No fixed hours. Field work concentrated in afternoons and evenings (when surveillance targets are home or out). Office hours technically posted, frequently ignored. Will work straight through a night on a hot case, then crash for fourteen hours.

**Evening.** Bar, often alone, sometimes with Malcolm or Cage. Specific bars on rotation — she does not become a regular at a single place, partly so no one can reliably find her, partly so no bartender starts asking after her. Three-to-five drinks is the modal session in the *Alias* era; less post-Danielle; she will still close a bar when triggered.

**Weekly.** No religious observance. No regular exercise (her physiology doesn't require it, which deprives her of a regulatory channel most trauma survivors benefit from). No therapy (notable; see §4). One quiet, near-ritualized weekly act: she walks past her parents' old Forest Hills address every few months, never stopping. This is inferential from her recurring Queens-orientation, but consistent with the pattern.

---

## 6.7 Signature Operational Tells

These are the items a longitudinal surveillance file would flag as **identifying behaviors** — the things she does that almost no one else does, that mark her work product as hers.

1. **The flat-affect cover story.** She does not modulate voice for pretexting. The cover is delivered in the same surly register as the truth, which is so unexpected that it works.
2. **The held silence in interview.** Two-to-five seconds longer than socially comfortable, then the subject speaks again, usually with the actual answer.
3. **The unconscious door-frame check.** Entering any room, eyes go to exits first, then to the largest body, then to hands. Reproducible across years of panel-level depiction.
4. **The purple-stimulus micro-freeze.** A half-second pause, often masked by a sip from whatever she's holding. She is aware of the tell and uses the sip to cover.
5. **The jacket.** The same leather jacket, repaired, identifiable. An adversary tracking her by silhouette has a stable target.
6. **Cash-and-walk-out at bars.** Pays in cash, leaves without saying goodbye. Avoids the closing-time conversation.
7. **Phone aversion.** Texts when she could call, doesn't call when she could text, drops calls mid-sentence when overwhelmed and lets the other party assume a connection problem.
8. **The deflective profanity.** When sincerely thanked or complimented, responds with a specific class of obscenity that closes the topic. Reliable enough to be a near-verbal-signature.
9. **The unilateral covert pivot.** Under sufficient stress, she stops communicating with allies and runs the operation alone. The pre-signal is usually 24–72 hours of unusual quietness followed by a fabricated reason to be unreachable.
10. **The protective intercession.** In any public space, she will physically place herself between a person being aggressed and the aggressor without speaking first. The body moves before the decision is verbalized.
11. **The Jewel-avoidance.** Will not say the name out loud if she can avoid it. If she must, says it with audible quotation marks. Will not be photographed in anything resembling the costume's color palette.
12. **The post-violence stillness.** After a fight she does not pace or vent — she sits down, often on the floor, and is quiet for several minutes. Where most trauma-physiology drives motion, hers drives collapse. This is the closest she comes to a regulatory practice.

---

## 6.8 Operational Strengths, Limits, and Exploit Surface

**Strengths.** Pattern recognition under volume. Tolerance for sustained ugliness — she will look at things others avert from. Power-tier sufficient to render most threats moot. A network whose nodes will move for her in ways no algorithm predicts. The credibility, with victims, of someone who is obviously also one. Absence of vanity, which removes the most common manipulation vector.

**Limits.** Digital and forensic tradecraft are dated. Physical fighting is untrained. Sleep and substance regulation are chronically poor, degrading judgment in long operations. Asks for help only after the optimal window has closed. Has no medical or psychiatric continuity of care, so injuries (somatic and psychological) accumulate undocumented.

**Exploit surface (for an adversary, mapped so a protector can pre-empt).** Purple-coded stimuli. Threats to Danielle (entirely predictable response, the one place she can be steered). Engineered scenarios in which she appears to be the villain to people whose regard she values (the 2016 wound shape — she will burn through allies trying to prove the negative). Sustained, structured kindness from a high-status figure (she does not have a response repertoire for it and will either flee or attach poorly). Offers of help phrased as instructions (Killgrave-coded; produces refusal even when the help is needed). And, prosaically: a sufficiently determined person who simply waits her out at any of three or four bars in lower Manhattan will find her within a week.

---

*End §6. Continue to §7 (Relational Mapping) for granular treatment of the Cage, Ducasse, Danvers, and Killgrave dyads.*


## Worldview & Operational Code

# WORLDVIEW & OPERATIONAL CODE

*UATU Dossier — Section 7 of 14. Subject: Jessica Campbell Jones.*

---

## 7.0 Preface: What "Worldview" Means in This File

A worldview, properly mapped, is not a creed the subject would recite. It is the *latent grammar* underneath thousands of micro-decisions: how she chooses a bar stool (back to the wall, eyeline on the door), how she phrases a question to a witness (declarative-with-bait rather than interrogative), what she does in the half-second after someone says "thank you" (looks away, deflects with profanity, exits the room within ninety seconds). Across a quarter-century of canonical observation the subject's grammar has stabilized into a coherent — if largely unspoken — operational philosophy. What follows is the reconstruction of that grammar, distilled from behavior and only secondarily from her sparse direct statements.

She would deny most of it. She would call it bullshit, then act, within the hour, in perfect accordance with every clause.

---

## 7.1 Metaphysics: The Shape of the World as She Reads It

**The world is not malevolent; it is indifferent and unevenly weighted.** The subject does not believe in cosmic justice, karmic ledger, or a moral universe. She also does not believe in active evil as a metaphysical category. She believes the world contains *power gradients* — between people, between institutions and individuals, between adults and children, between predators and prey — and that almost all suffering she has personally seen reduces to someone with more power exercising it on someone with less, while everyone else looks away. Her cosmology is therefore not theological but mechanical: gravity wells of capability, around which everything else orbits.

**There is no plan.** A road can have a truck with chemicals on it and a family in a station wagon, and the chemicals can spill in one direction rather than another, and that is the whole story. She has accepted, at a level deeper than grief, that meaning is something humans bolt onto outcomes after the fact. This is why she resists, with reflexive ferocity, anyone who tries to tell her that her suffering "made her who she is" or that "everything happens for a reason." Such framings are, in her ear, obscene — they retroactively justify the perpetrators. The accident did not happen *for* anything. The captivity did not happen *for* anything. They simply happened, and now there is a person standing in the wreckage with a job to do.

**Most institutions are inert at best.** SHIELD did not find her in Killgrave's apartment. The Avengers did not visit the hospital. The Bugle pays her to dignify Jameson's hostilities. Hospitals discharge people without checking what they're going home to. This is not, in her reading, a conspiracy — it is the structural fact that institutions are optimized for institutional survival, not for individual rescue, and anyone waiting for an institution to show up at the moment of need is going to wait a long time. She will *use* institutions tactically (a SHIELD badge opens doors; an Avengers number gets a call returned) and despises them sentimentally.

**Costumes are mostly vanity, occasionally function.** This is the heretical core of her professional aesthetic. She does not believe most superheroes are bad people; she believes most of them are people who have not examined the relationship between the spectacle of their work and the work itself. The cape is a *statement,* and statements are for an audience, and audiences distort. The PI license, by contrast, is for the work. (She makes exceptions — Cage's hoodie and chain were always functional; Murdock's costume she reads as part-armor part-theology; Carol's uniform is military and she respects military things grudgingly. The exceptions prove the rule by being legible to her as *non-vain*.)

---

## 7.2 Epistemology: How She Decides What Is True

**Words are unreliable; actions over time are the only valid evidence.** This is the most consequential single belief in her operating system, and it is downstream of Killgrave. Killgrave's words made her body do things her mind did not want. Words, therefore, are not data; they are *pressure.* When a client tells her something, she logs the sentence as a behavior — *this person chose to say this, to me, now, for some reason* — and treats the content as one hypothesis among many. When Cage says he loves her, she does not believe the sentence; she believes the seven years of him showing up. When Carol apologizes, she does not absorb the apology; she watches what Carol does in the next month.

**Pattern over instance.** A single observation proves nothing; a trajectory proves almost everything. She is patient about evidence in a way that surprises people who read her as impulsive. She will sit on a case for weeks, accumulating small behavioral data — what time the subject leaves the apartment, which side of the elevator they stand on, whether they tip — and decline to act until the pattern coheres. This is professional craft, but it is also epistemic doctrine: people *are* what they repeatedly do, and what they repeatedly do can be observed if you are willing to be bored long enough.

**Self-report is the lowest tier of evidence — including her own.** She does not trust what people say about themselves, and she extends this skepticism inward. She is aware that her own narration of her motives is unreliable, that she lies to herself fluently, and that her affect lags her cognition. She therefore tends to act first and theorize later, treating her behavior as the more honest readout of what she actually wants. (This is why she could not articulate her feelings for Cage for years but kept showing up at his door.)

**The body knows first.** Her hypervigilance has been calibrated by trauma into a fast-twitch threat detector that is right considerably more often than chance. She has learned, slowly, to treat the prickle at the back of the neck as actionable intelligence rather than pathology. The danger is that the same system fires false positives in any room that contains: purple lighting, a man with a smooth voice, an over-attentive stranger, a closed door she did not close. She has developed a meta-rule: trust the somatic alarm enough to *leave or reposition,* not enough to *attack.*

**Conspiracy is rare; coincidence and incompetence are common.** Despite operating in a universe that genuinely contains Skrull infiltrations and Hand resurrections, she defaults to the boring explanation. Cheating spouses are usually cheating. Missing people are usually depressed or in debt. The cape stuff intrudes, but she does not look for it first; she rules out the mundane and only then reaches for the exotic. This makes her unusually effective in a city full of investigators who reach for the exotic first.

---

## 7.3 Theory of Power and Control

**Power is morally neutral; the question is whether the holder has examined it.** She does not think strength is bad. She is strong. She thinks *unexamined* power — strength wielded by someone who has not asked themselves what it does to the person on the other end — is the engine of most harm. Killgrave is the limit case: a man whose power *is* the absence of examination, because his victims cannot push back to teach him. Every adult, in her schema, has some power over someone — a parent over a child, a boss over an employee, a journalist over a subject, a hero over a civilian — and the moral question is always whether they remember it.

**Control is the cardinal sin.** Not violence, not deception, not even cruelty in isolation: *control.* The thing that makes Killgrave the worst person she has ever encountered is not what he made her do but that he made her *want* to do it — he annexed the territory of her own consent. Everything she classifies as evil shares this structure: a person who reaches past another person's will and operates the controls directly. This is why she has near-allergic reactions to: being "handled" by Avengers leadership, being "managed" by Cage when he means well, being prescribed a course of action by Carol "for her own good," being lied to by people who think she can't handle the truth. The content of the intervention is irrelevant. The *form* — someone else reaching into her decision-space — is the violation.

**Coercion exists on a continuum, and most of it is invisible to its practitioners.** She has the survivor's calibrated eye for soft coercion: the friend who frames a request as a foregone conclusion, the boss who praises in order to extract, the lover who sulks until compliance, the institution that offers help with strings. She tracks this constantly. She is often the only person in a room who notices that a "request" is a demand. She rarely names it aloud; she just resists, and people who don't understand the resistance read her as difficult.

**Consent is the only metaphysics she fully believes in.** Strip away the cynicism and the drinking and what is left is a near-sacred conception of consent — the right of a being to decide, in their own body, what they will do and to whom. It is the only thing she will fight reliably for, in herself or others. It is why she protects victims with disproportionate ferocity. It is why she could not look at the Jewel costume after Killgrave: the costume had been her free choice, and he had taken even that backward into himself, turned it into a uniform he made her wear. Returning to consent — small acts of reclaimed choice, day by day — is, though she would never frame it this way, her recovery project.

---

## 7.4 Theory of Trust and Relationship

**Trust is earned slowly, in a thousand small installments, and revoked instantly on certain specific violations.** The violations that revoke trust are not the obvious ones (lying, even significant lying, can be metabolized if the motive is legible). The unforgivable category is: *taking a decision out of her hands while claiming to act in her interest.* Carol triggered this in the 2016 arc. Cage has triggered it, recoverably, several times. The Avengers institutionally have triggered it permanently. Once triggered, the offender is not eliminated from her life but is moved into a new tier — present but not consulted, useful but not believed.

**Relationships are not symmetric and pretending they are is dangerous.** She does not believe in the egalitarian fiction that all parties in a relationship hold the same kind of power. In every dyad she enters, she maps the power gradient first: who can hurt whom, who needs whom more, who can leave more easily. This is not cynicism; it is the safety briefing she performs before staying. She accepts asymmetries (Cage is physically stronger; she is psychologically more agile; Danielle is wholly dependent) and her ethical work is in *not exploiting* the asymmetries she could exploit.

**Chosen family is the only family that survives scrutiny.** Her biological family is dead. Her adoptive family is, in her felt experience, a kindly accident. The people she calls family in the operational sense are the ones who passed her filter and stayed: Cage, Danielle, Malcolm, and — across long oscillations — Carol. She is more loyal to this set than she is to any other commitment, including the law, including her own safety, and she does not advertise this fact because advertising it would invite leverage.

**Most people will not show up. The few who will, you keep.** This is the operational summary. She has no expectation that the average bystander will intervene, that the average institution will respond, that the average friend will stay. She is therefore unsurprised by failure and disproportionately moved by the rare reliable presence. Malcolm bringing her coffee in the morning has, over time, become a piece of evidence about the structure of the universe.

**Intimacy is exposure, and exposure is dangerous, and the danger does not go away — you just decide some people are worth it.** She has not arrived at the comforting view that love is safe. She has arrived at the harder view that love is *worth the unsafety,* in specific cases, for specific people, with eyes open. This is the most adult position in her belief system and the one she has worked hardest for.

---

## 7.5 Theory of Conflict

**Avoid the fight you can't win, and most fights you can't win.** She is not, despite the strength, a brawler by disposition. She has been beaten unconscious by people stronger than her (the Vision, Iron Man) and she has internalized the lesson: raw capability is a poor predictor of who walks out of the room. She prefers to *not enter the room,* and when entering is required, to enter it on terms she has chosen — angle of approach, timing, who else is present, what the egress is.

**When you do fight, end it.** Once committed, she does not pull punches and does not negotiate mid-conflict. She has watched, in her own life, what hesitation costs. She fights briefly, conclusively, and with the minimum theater. She is uninterested in the choreography of combat and is contemptuous of opponents who treat fights as performance.

**The first weapon is composure.** This is the lesson from her victory over Killgrave in *Alias.* He won by destabilizing her affect — by making her want, fear, beg. She won by refusing to be baited, by holding the steady center while he escalated. She has generalized this: in any high-stakes encounter, the participant who can hold their own affect longest controls the encounter, regardless of who is physically stronger. Her drinking, in this light, is not only self-medication but also *practice* — she has spent years training herself to function while her chemistry is hostile, which is exactly the skill the Killgrave-grade adversary requires.

**Violence is a tool of last resort, but it is in fact a tool.** She is not a pacifist. She has thrown men through walls. She will do it again. What she refuses is *redemptive* or *expressive* violence — violence as catharsis, as message, as identity. Violence is for stopping a specific harm to a specific person, and when the harm has stopped, the violence stops.

**De-escalation is not virtue; it is competence.** She does not de-escalate because she is gentle; she de-escalates because escalated situations are unpredictable and unpredictable situations get bystanders killed. She has a small toolkit for taking the temperature out of a room — going still, dropping her voice, offering an unexpected concession on a small point, sometimes a deliberately disarming profanity. None of it is taught; all of it is field-acquired.

---

## 7.6 Theory of Risk

**Risk to self is cheap; risk to others is expensive.** This is the asymmetric calculus that drives most of her tactical choices. She will take large personal risks — physical, legal, reputational — because she has internally devalued her own welfare and because she trusts her own competence to absorb the consequence. She will take *no* avoidable risk with Danielle, with Malcolm, with a client, with a stranger in the wrong place at the wrong time. This produces the characteristic pattern of her late-period work: she goes alone, she lies to her allies about going alone, and she accepts disproportionate cost rather than expose anyone she loves to the splash.

**The worst outcome is not death; it is being controlled, or watching someone you love be controlled.** Her risk hierarchy departs from the standard one. Many operators rank: death > maiming > capture > defeat. Her ranking is: *loss of will* > harm to her child > harm to her chosen family > capture > maiming > death > defeat > embarrassment. This is why she will accept jail (2016 arc) to prevent a Killgrave-coded outcome; why she would die before being mind-controlled again; why she would surrender to anyone to keep Danielle safe. Understanding this hierarchy is essential to predicting her — she will look reckless to anyone using the conventional ranking and entirely consistent to anyone using hers.

**Sunk cost is not a reason to continue, but it is a reason to finish.** She distinguishes between continuing a doomed plan because of what has been spent (she will not) and finishing an obligation she has accepted because she said she would (she will, often past the point of self-interest). The distinction is moral, not economic: her word, once given to a client or a friend, is one of the few things she still believes in, and finishing the case is how she honors it.

**Hope is a risk to be managed.** She does not consider hope a virtue. She considers it a high-variance bet that has, in her life, paid out catastrophically. She permits herself small, structured hopes — that Danielle will sleep through the night, that this case will close clean, that Cage will be home by Tuesday — and resists large, structural ones. This is not depression; it is portfolio management.

---

## 7.7 Theory of Morality

**There is no rulebook; there is only the next decision.** She is not a deontologist. She has no list of inviolable principles she could enumerate. She has, instead, a small set of *tests* she runs on a contemplated action: *Does this take a choice away from someone? Does this protect the weaker party in the room? Will I be able to look at this in the morning? Would I do this if no one were watching, including me?* The tests are not a system; they are a practice.

**Guilt is reliable signal; shame is not.** She has come, painfully, to distinguish between *guilt* (I did a specific thing that was wrong; I can name it; I can repair or learn from it) and *shame* (I am wrong; I am contaminated; nothing can repair it). Guilt she trusts and acts on. Shame she has identified as the residue of Killgrave and tries — with mixed success — to refuse. The distinction is recent in her development and still effortful.

**Forgiveness is not owed and is not the point.** She does not believe in forgiveness as a moral imperative. She does not forgive Killgrave; she does not forgive the Avengers; she does not forgive the friends and institutions that failed her. What she does is *decide whether to continue the relationship,* which is a separate question. Some people she continues with (Cage, Carol) without ever issuing forgiveness; the relationship survives because both parties keep showing up, not because a debt has been formally cleared. She finds the Christian framing of forgiveness — the obligation of the wronged to absolve — quietly offensive, because it again locates the work in the victim.

**The moral floor is not heroic; it is minimal.** She does not think she is a good person. She thinks she is a person who has a few things she will not do under any circumstance: she will not become Killgrave; she will not abandon a child; she will not take a paycheck to hurt someone who didn't earn it; she will not sell out the few people who showed up for her. The list is short on purpose. A short list of inviolable items, she has discovered, is more reliable than a long list of aspirational ones.

---

## 7.8 Theory of Self

**She is not "Jewel" and she is not "the woman Killgrave made."** The hardest construction project of her adulthood has been carving out a self that is neither the pre-trauma naïf nor the post-trauma object. The self she has assembled is functional but provisional — *Jessica Jones, investigator, partner of Luke, mother of Danielle, friend of Malcolm, occasional irritant of Carol.* It is built from roles she has chosen and relationships she has earned, not from an essence she believes she possesses. She is suspicious of any account of her that reaches for essence.

**Her competence is real; her worth is contested.** She trusts that she is good at her job. This is one of the few stable beliefs she holds about herself, and it survives drinking, depression, and bad weeks. She does not trust that she is *worth* the affection she receives, and she does not expect this question to be resolved in her lifetime. She has decided, instead, to act as if she were worth it — to receive the love provisionally, on credit — because the alternative (refusing it) hurts the people offering it, and hurting them is a violation of her actual operating code.

**The Jewel costume in the closet is not nostalgia or shame; it is evidence.** She has not destroyed it. The fact that she has not destroyed it is, in this analyst's reading, the most hopeful single data point in her file. It is the artifact of a self that existed before Killgrave and survived him. She does not wear it. She does not look at it often. But she keeps it, because to throw it away would be to concede that he won the argument about who she was before he met her, and on this point she has decided, somewhere below articulation, that he does not get to win.

**She is, by her own private accounting, a survivor first and a hero never.** She will not accept the word "hero" applied to herself. She will, with great reluctance, accept "survivor," and only because the word is descriptive rather than honorific. If pressed (she will not be pressed) she would say her job is to be the person in the room who has already been through it and is therefore harder to scare. That, to her, is not heroism. That is just what one becomes if one declines to die.

---

## 7.9 Operational Code: The Short Form

Distilled, the code she actually runs reduces to a small number of operational rules. She has never written them down. She does not know she follows them. The file infers them from a quarter-century of behavior:

1. **Watch first. Act late. Finish completely.**
2. **Believe behavior, not speech — including your own.**
3. **Never take a choice out of someone's hands, including when you are sure you are right.**
4. **Protect the weaker party in the room. Identify them in the first ten seconds.**
5. **Hold your own affect. The one who holds longest controls the room.**
6. **Spend yourself before you spend anyone else.**
7. **Keep the list of inviolables short. Defend it absolutely.**
8. **Guilt is data. Shame is noise. Sort them every morning.**
9. **The few who show up, you keep. The rest, you note and move past.**
10. **Do not become him.**

The tenth rule is unspoken and load-bearing. Every other rule is, in the final analysis, a sub-routine of it.

---

*End Section 7. Cross-reference Section 8 (Daily Rituals & Micro-Behaviors) for the granular conduct in which this code is executed, and Section 11 (Decompensation Profile) for the failure modes when the code cannot hold.*


## Moral & Ethical Framework

# MORAL & ETHICAL FRAMEWORK

## Preamble: the difference between her stated code and her enacted code

Jessica Jones is, professionally and conversationally, a moral nihilist. She talks like someone who does not believe in goodness — capes are vain, institutions are corrupt, people are mostly garbage, she herself is worse than most of them, the work is just work, the rent is just the rent. This is the *stated* code, and it is almost entirely defensive packaging. The *enacted* code is unusually rigorous, unusually consistent, and substantially more demanding than the codes of most of the costumed peers she sneers at. The gap between the two is itself a moral datum: she has chosen to be a person whose actions are better than her self-presentation, which is the precise inverse of the standard heroic vanity she finds intolerable. Document the enacted code; treat the stated code as one of her defenses.

What follows is reconstructed from observed choices across roughly two decades of canon — what she actually does when the file is on her desk and no one is watching.

---

## 1. The first principle: protect the smaller party in the room

The bedrock rule, derivable from every period of her operational life. Whoever in any given encounter has less power — physical, social, financial, informational, developmental — is the party whose interests she will weight above her own, above her client's, and above the law. She does not articulate this as a principle; she enacts it so consistently it functions as one.

Operational signatures:
- In cheating-spouse cases (*Alias* passim) she will repeatedly take the side of the spouse who was lied to over the client who hired her, even when this means losing the fee, sandbagging the report, or warning the target.
- In the Mattie Franklin / MGH arc she abandons the paying engagement the moment she realizes the missing young woman is being chemically and sexually exploited; the rest of the case becomes pro bono extraction work she conducts at personal cost, against people who outweigh her.
- In the small-town Captain America-impersonator case she sides with the closeted teenager over the entire community structure paying her, despite this requiring her to remain in the very Americana environment that re-traumatizes her.
- With Danielle, the principle becomes total: the smallest party in any room she enters is now permanently her daughter, and every tactical calculation reroutes through that.

This rule has not shifted over time. It is the most stable element in her ethics and predates her recovery; it was operating during her worst drinking. It is, by her own implicit logic, what makes her not Killgrave — Killgrave is the person who exploits the weaker party in every room, and her code is structured as his exact photonegative.

## 2. The second principle: never override another person's will

Post-captivity, this becomes the load-bearing wall. She will not coerce, will not lie people into action they have not consented to, will not "handle" people for their own good, will not deploy her strength to override anyone's choice unless they are in the act of overriding someone else's. This is why she is so allergic to the Avengers operational style (which she experiences as institutional handling), to Jameson's editorial direction (which she experiences as moral coercion via payroll), and to Carol's well-meaning interventions in her drinking (which she experiences as the small civilian version of Killgrave's pheromone).

Granular consequences:
- She does not interrogate the way most investigators interrogate. She extracts information through patience, pattern, and surveillance rather than pressure, because pressure-on-the-unwilling is the Killgrave method scaled down.
- She telegraphs her presence to targets more than tradecraft requires. Several *Alias* cases would be cleaner if she ambushed; she chooses to confront, even at tactical cost, because ambush feels like the thing he did to her.
- She will not lie to Cage about anything operational that affects him directly — except (and the exception is the central fracture, addressed below) when she has convinced herself she is protecting him by overriding his choice to be informed.
- She refuses to use her strength to win arguments. She has never, in any depicted domestic scene, used physical intimidation against an intimate. With Killgrave-coded male predators she has no such compunction.

## 3. The third principle: the moral floor — what she will not do, drunk or sober

A short, stable list. She will not:
- Kill a non-combatant or surrendered party. (She has come close with Killgrave specifically and has not crossed it; the restraint is significant given provocation.)
- Take money from a client whose case she has not actually worked, even when she could.
- Use her powers for personal financial gain beyond the baseline (no robbery, no extortion, no leveraging cape access for cash — note her sustained near-poverty across *Alias*, which is a moral choice masquerading as failure).
- Out a person who is hiding for safety reasons. The closeted-teen case is the canonical example; she absorbs serious cost to protect a stranger's disclosure timeline.
- Coerce sex or sexual disclosure from a subject of investigation. She will sleep with people for her own reasons, never for the case.
- Hit someone weaker than her in anger, even when she's furious enough to want to. Her rage is loud; her hands, in those moments, are unusually disciplined.

This list has not changed in twenty years of publication. It is the irreducible spine.

## 4. The fourth principle: institutional non-compliance with selective cooperation

She does not believe in the legitimacy of institutions to set the moral frame, but she will work with them transactionally when the alternative is worse for the smaller party. This is the *contemptuous-compliant* register noted in the personality section, formalized into an ethic.

- She registers under the SHRA during *Civil War* — but reluctantly, framed entirely as a Danielle-protection move, and she flees the country first to make sure compliance is on her terms.
- She takes the *Pulse* job under Jameson knowing he is exploitative, because the platform makes some cases reachable that otherwise are not.
- She works with SHIELD, the Avengers, the NYPD, the Bar — but never accepts their framing of who the bad guy is, and routinely flips on them mid-engagement when their frame collides with principle one.

She is not an anti-authoritarian on principle; she is a particularist who has never met an authority she trusts to apply principle one correctly. The consistency is in the underlying rule, not the institutional posture.

## 5. The fifth principle: truth-telling as a moral category, with one specified exception

Killgrave taught her that words can be weapons; her response has been to treat her own words with unusual moral seriousness. She lies far less than her affect suggests. The cynical-PI register is performance; the underlying speech act is more honest than most of her peers'. She will:
- Tell clients what she actually found, even when they are paying her to find something else.
- Tell Cage uncomfortable truths about herself with a brutality she does not extend to others.
- Refuse to flatter, to reassure falsely, or to perform sentiment she does not feel.

The exception — and it is the principal *fracture* in the entire code — is the *2016 deep-cover gambit*: she manufactures a separation from Cage, allows Carol to believe she has decompensated, accepts jail, and lies systematically to everyone who loves her for the duration of a Purple-Children-adjacent operation. She justifies this as protect-the-smaller-party (the children, possibly Danielle herself) and as the only way to operate inside a network that would detect any tell. But it is, examined closely, a violation of principle two against the very people whose consent she is most obligated to honor. She overrode Cage's and Carol's right to be informed because she had decided she knew better. This is the precise structure of the Killgrave move, in miniature, and the arc functions as her unconscious staging of that recognition. She does not, notably, repent of the choice. She integrates it.

## 6. Consistencies (where the code holds even under maximum load)

- **Under intoxication.** The moral floor (section 3) holds during her worst drinking. Drunk Jessica is meaner, sloppier, more reckless with her own safety — but does not cross the floor. This is rare and worth flagging; substance-driven decompensation usually erodes ethical performance proportionally. Hers does not. The floor is structural, not state-dependent.
- **Under threat to self.** Multiple instances of accepting beatings, jail, public humiliation rather than betray a principle. The Vision/Iron Man beating she did not deserve, the post-Killgrave hospital silence, the 2016 incarceration — she has a high pain-tolerance for being unjustly punished, which is itself a moral capacity (and a trauma-derived one: she already survived the worst version of being misread).
- **Under social pressure to perform heroism.** She refuses, repeatedly, to play the role expected of a powered person — and this refusal is itself ethically motivated, not merely defensive. She believes performing heroism corrupts it; she will not give the audience what flatters her.

## 7. Fractures (where the code breaks or bends)

- **The Cage deception (2016), as above.** The clearest fracture. She justifies the lie via a higher principle (protection) but the structure replicates the wound. Integration without repentance suggests the code accommodates a narrow utilitarian override under sufficiently extreme conditions — which means the absolutism of principle two is qualified, and she will not admit by how much.
- **Self-directed cruelty.** The code prohibits coercion of others; it does not prohibit her treatment of herself. She permits, against herself, what she would prevent against any client: degradation, neglect, hazardous use, sexual self-punishment. The asymmetry is not accidental — it is the shame-organized self at work — but it is an ethical inconsistency she has not closed. (She has narrowed it over the parenting period: she takes better care of herself because Danielle is downstream of her body. Even her self-care is other-directed.)
- **Killgrave as the asymptote.** She has not killed him. She will not kill him. But she has rehearsed it, and the restraint costs her more than she admits. The code holds, but barely, and only because killing him would, in her private logic, retroactively make the captivity into the thing that turned her into him. The principle is sound; the motive is partly self-protective rather than purely ethical.
- **Selective application of principle two within domestic life.** She honors others' will scrupulously in public; in close relationships she occasionally substitutes withdrawal-without-explanation for honest disclosure, which is a soft form of overriding the other's right to participate in the relationship. Cage has, fairly, named this.
- **Cape contempt as moral cover.** Her dismissal of capes is partly principled (vanity critique) and partly defensive (she cannot bear to be compared and found wanting). The principled portion is real; the defensive portion sometimes lets her off ethical hooks she would not let others off — e.g., declining collaborations that would have produced better outcomes for vulnerable parties because she did not want to be in the room with people in costume. The code, here, is contaminated by the shame.

## 8. Justifications: the structure of how she reasons morally

She does not reason consequentially in the standard utilitarian sense. She reasons in something closer to a *particularist deontology* organized around the figure of the specific vulnerable person in front of her. The rule is not "maximize welfare" and not "follow the categorical imperative"; the rule is closer to *do not be, in this moment, the kind of thing he was*.
